========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] final vowels
From: "Jay Bowks" <jjbowks{at}adam{dot}cheshire{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 12 Jul 1999 19:20:17 -0400

From: Paul O. Bartlett <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
>    Then let's scrap Glosa (and every other constructed auxlang) 
>and have everybody speak English.  After all, English is the most 
>successful auxiliary language in history.  Why not stick with a 
>winner?

As a teacher of foreign languages to English
speakers I can truly say that American (or other
wise native English speakers) can and do 
pronounce vowels well enough to be totally
and thoroughly comprehensible to native
speakers of languages that have clear sounding
final vowels. 

And also...

One of the reasons why Spanish is very
comprehensible to Portuguese speakers 
is the clarity of the vowels as opposed to
the other case where Portuguese is hard
for Speakers of Spanish who are not 
used to the long and short vowels of
Portuguese... eg. "casa" as "kah-zuh"
instead of "kah-sah" etc. To many Spanish
speakers the Brazilian variety is a lot more
comprehensible than the Portuguese 
mainland accent or the Azorean accent
one finds in the U.S. on the East Coast
quite often.

To use unstressed vowels in an IAL is
a big mistake in my opinion, since they
add nothing to the recognizability of words
and add a lot to their being hard to master.

Glosa is not Volapuk, and the umlauted
sort of sounds although easy for an Anglo
or German speaker are not so for others.

As to whether English is the most successful
auxiliary language... this is true, but on the
other hand it is also lots of fun to communicate
in something else. "Glosaists have more fun!"
Now if we could come up with a way to 
convince everyone of this we'd have an easy
time wouldn't we?

Since,
Jay B.

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] dictionary
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick_hempshall{at}hotmail{dot}com>
Date: Tue, 1 Aug 2000 02:11:34 +0100

Marcel Springer grafo (mikri mero)

> 4. Problems for a German
> ------------------------
>
> 4.1 It is not easy to distinguish between:
>       hekasto (each, every) / panto (all) / holo (hole) /
>         (G1000:) singu.
> 4.2 The German does not distinguish:
>       ali (any) / uno (some)
>     ... and an international auxiliary language MUST NOT do either!!!


~panto~ is "all" when there is a group of things being talked about [alle].
~Panto fito nece gene foto.~

~holo~ is "all" when there is one thing and you are talking about the whole
thing [ganz, all]. ~Mi vora (un) holo plaka.~

~singu~ is for when some things are distributed among a number of other
things [jede]. ~Singu manu habe penta digi~

~hekasto~ I guess, is a synonym for ~panto~


I agree that "any" and "some" should not be distinguished, so ~ali~ and
~uno~ can be synonyms

Nick

Karo Charles, 

Charles grafo 00-07-31 
(about "English 5000 to Glosa 1000" vocabulary list in the GEO-book
"Central Glosa"): 
 > Marcel's sample of English words beginning in RO:
 >   roam, roar, roast, ... rove, row boat, row, royal
 > Kilgariff's words in RO: 
 >   ... 
 > So by my rough count, the M-list includes 19 of 25 K-items. 
 > This approximates a good practical word list for English-Glosa.
 > We could ...

Funny, Charles, I played around with some statistics, too.  Perhaps 
I was inspired by your previous mails. 

I took out my old school book ("Grund- und Aufbauwortschatz Englisch", 
Klett Vl., Stuttgart 1977).  It goes back to "General Service List of 
English Words" by Michael West, 1953.  Charles, do you know this list? 
Who is "better", West or Kilgariff?  The book contains 
"Grund"-vocabulary 2000 words that should enable a text understanding 
of 85 % and "Aufbau", another 2500 words, should enable to 95 %.  The 
small list "roam ... royal" from "E5000 G1000" contains all of "Grund" 
(except "road" and "ring" (sound)) and most of "Aufbau".  So 
"English 5000 to Glosa 1000" can be estimated near to 90 %.  

Could it be, that ye ole, silly Glosa 1000, so much shouted at by us 
here in Glosalist, is not so bad? 

If so, or nearly so, we could: 
- Take Bill Patterson's dictionary 
    (www.neosplice.com/~ailanto/glosa.htm) and 
- put in "English 5000 to Glosa 1000". 
    (Unfortunately printing quality of the "Central Glosa" book is so 
    bad, that scanning and OCR-text recognition fails.) 
- Make again 3 levels: "Literary" with all words, "Base" without 
    synonyms, but this time prefer Glosa 1000 words (of course 
    without hidri/aqa, kron/tem, hipo/sub ...) and "Core" for 
    the most useful words, chosen with help of Kilgariff or similar.
Then:
- Create missing words, if needed. 
- Fix errors I mentioned 00-07-28.
- Contact Wendy. 
- Solve the bad synonym problem (karo/kara, silva/silvi ...) and other 
    ending vowel problems (krisi (crisis)/ kriso (golden)). 
- Refine the vocabulary by comparison with other Romanic languages, 
    other world languages and other IALs. 
- Discuss the results in Glosalist. 
- Translate the dictionary into French, German, Russian, Spain, ... 
- Test out the vocabulary by practical use: communication, 
    translations, writing literature. 

A long way.  But quickly a status can be reached that is better than 
the current.

The bad ones are bali/bale, different words with similar roots.
The ones like silva/silvi, same word spelled slightly different,
are a much easier thing to fix. Yes, we should fix them for the
new list, i.e. not include both bale/bali, and spell silva with -a.

Marcel Springer grafo (mero)

> I took out my old school book ("Grund- und Aufbauwortschatz Englisch",
> Klett Vl., Stuttgart 1977).  It goes back to "General Service List of
> English Words" by Michael West, 1953.  Charles, do you know this list?
> Who is "better", West or Kilgariff?  The book contains
> "Grund"-vocabulary 2000 words that should enable a text understanding
> of 85 % and "Aufbau", another 2500 words, should enable to 95 %.  The
> small list "roam ... royal" from "E5000 G1000" contains all of "Grund"
> (except "road" and "ring" (sound)) and most of "Aufbau".  So
> "English 5000 to Glosa 1000" can be estimated near to 90 %.
>
> Could it be, that ye ole, silly Glosa 1000, so much shouted at by us
> here in Glosalist, is not so bad?

No, it's looking good.

>
> If so, or nearly so, we could:
> - Take Bill Patterson's dictionary
>     (www.neosplice.com/~ailanto/glosa.htm) and
> - put in "English 5000 to Glosa 1000".
>     (Unfortunately printing quality of the "Central Glosa" book is so
>     bad, that scanning and OCR-text recognition fails.)
> - Make again 3 levels: "Literary" with all words, "Base" without
>     synonyms, but this time prefer Glosa 1000 words (of course
>     without hidri/aqa, kron/tem, hipo/sub ...) and "Core" for
>     the most useful words, chosen with help of Kilgariff or similar.

Note: there is a distinction between ~kron~ [instant] and ~tem~ [period].

~Mi vo vide an; kron an veni~  [I want to see him, when he comes]   i.e. at
the moment of his arrival

~Mi vo vide an; tem an veni~  [I want to see him, while he comes]   i.e.
during his journey

~Mi pa es to kolegi tem mo horo~ [I was at college for one hour]

~Mi pa es lo rura kron mo horo~ [I was in the country-side at one o'clock]

Note the similar distinction between ~lo~,~loka~ [a place] (i.e. space
extended) and ~to~,~topo~ [a spot] (i.e. space restricted)

> A long way.  But quickly a status can be reached that is better than
> the current.

Sounds like we have a good way forward.

Nick

final vowel to be chosen for euphony, personal preference, or even as a way
of demonstrating some attitude the speaker/writer may have towards the
utterance (truthfulness, value, agreement, emotion, etc.)?

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] final vowels
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 01 Aug 2000 21:05:40 -0700

	
> A suggestion, designed to stimulate comment rather than immediate
> acceptance:

Oh, that won't save you from the flames ...

> why not make the Glosa word really the "root" and leave the
> final vowel to be chosen for euphony, personal preference, or even as a way
> of demonstrating some attitude the speaker/writer may have towards the
> utterance (truthfulness, value, agreement, emotion, etc.)?

My guess is that English-speakers will only pronounce
-i -a -o decently. And it won't hurt anything at all.
Pick the most plausible etymological vowel and schwa it.

But the Ido speakers might faithfully carry over their
-a for adjectives, -o for nouns, -e for adverbs. Beware,
they will try -i for plurals too! (I formerly urged the use
of part-of-speech marking for Glosa, but not anymore.)

It could be a lot of fun, actually, to watch it evolve.
Final vowels as fashion accessories?

tem2; andro sti akti de dona*
tem3; andro kolekti avi*
tem4; andro pa dona a gina*

tem0   tem1   tem2   tem3  tem4
       andro  dona   avi   a gina

N V O IO es u deko modo de akorda verba seri ko tem seri.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] tem e gramatika
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Wed, 02 Aug 2000 13:52:47 -0700


> tem0; il es andro, avi e gina. Nuli-ra acide.
> tem1; andro* pa skema
> tem2; andro sti akti de dona*
> tem3; andro kolekti avi*
> tem4; andro pa dona a gina*
> 
> tem0   tem1   tem2   tem3  tem4
>        andro  dona   avi   a gina
> 
> N V O IO es u deko modo de akorda verba seri ko tem seri.

That is the usual "unmarked" order, where the subject N
is the same as the agent/doer/causer role, and it matches
temporal cause-effect order as well, very useful.

Now, trying to imagine a conversation, ...
"An pa dona avi a qo-pe?" ... "A gina."
The examples in the reference seem to forbid these:
"A GINA an dona un avi." "A GINA un andro dona un avi."
Glosa doesn't invert word order for questions, either.

But not many languages forbid "fronting" for topicalization,
which is almost inevitable. One might try something like this
to emphasize the agent/doer: "PER ANDRO, gina pa gene un avi".

Ido uses "na" as a preposition for the direct object,
Otto Jesperson recommended "em" as an improvement.
With this there's much less need for clumsy passives,
and one annoying/superfluous article can be dropped;
"EM AVI un andro pa dona a gina."

So using PER/AD/EM seems to allow more flexibility and
expressiveness, at the usual cost of complexity. But maybe
removal or avoidance of passives makes it worthwhile?
The idea (originally Syd's, IIRC) seems attractive.

A popular idea in linguistic analysis called "optimality"
describes the tendency of language to balance certain goals
against others; the agent/doer tends to the front/left of the
sentence, competing in vain against a topicalized phrase,
which has higher precedence, but the agent out-competes
objects (unless they get topicalized).

Earlier-in-time is generally front/left-ward of later, so
most (95%) languages are SO rather than OS. Similar forces
lead to either prepositions and VO, or inflections and OV.
The only odd thing about Glosa is its adjective-noun order,
which tends (but not strongly) to go with OV languages.

Anyway, if fronting is to be allowed then we will need "em".

and

> language was fairly 'natural.'
>       Writing scientific reports in Glosa is very dry and very logical:
> perhaps Glosa could be described as "techno-speak."
>       So, there we go: if the idea of a language being intuitive, and
> logical, have any validity, then so has Glosa.  While designing the
> 'speak-as-you-look-it-up-in-the-dictionary' language, Ron Clark also had
> the good sense to ensure that both the appearance and the sound of the
> phrasing was pleasant, as well as making sense.
>       Regarding the rules, and there is basically only one - a word is
> modified by its precedent - Ron was horrified at the thought that learners
> should sit down and internalise a grammar book of rules; children
certainly
> don't.  So Ron decreed that people would speak Glosa without first
studying
> a Grammar book.  In fact, he virtually banned the idea of producing such a
> guide.  This was, perhaps his biggest mistake: he uvastly over-estimated
> the initiative that could be shown by those making up the bulk of the
human
> race.  For research purposes, and against the wishes of Ron and Wendy, I
> did set up a tabulation of Glosa's grammar, but this was purely
> descriptive, and not prescriptive.
>       While this tabulation of Glosa's grammar is in reasonable shape, I
> would NOT recommend first 'learning the grammar' so that you could 'apply'
> the rules when you spoke or wrote Glosa.  I would follow Ron in suggesting
> that learners understood enough of the philosophy behind the language so
> they could learn by 'jumping-in-the-deep-end' and

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 5 Aug 2000 14:08:04 +0930 (CST)

At 2000-07-31 18:29 +1000, Robin Gaskell wrote:

> At 12:02 26/07/2000 +0930, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:
> >
> >> I like the idea of a pure-ideal pidgin/creole as IAL.
> >
> >Using separate words in place of the number inflexion of the noun
> >or the tense inflexions of the verb seems perfectly obvious and
> >easy.  But I'm not certain that there is any advantage in it.  
> >
> *   It can depend on cultural and psychological factors, and may not have
> anything to do with logic.
>     However, there are patterns in common in English and the other more
> successful national languages: we are still struggling with the idea of a
> "Universal Grammar."

I don't know whether I'm saying anything new to most people here,
but I recently noticed the Radio Australia Tok Pisin service at
http://www.abc.net.au/ra/newsratp/.  A large resource.

Tok Pisin does seem to be a pidgin rather than a
language.  I.e. what you see is English with simplified grammar
assisted by a characteristic idiom that heavily depends on
"bilong" (belong) and "long" (alongside, I think, a legacy, as
Charles suggests, of contact with sailors).  And a simplified
phonetic spelling which largely emulates the pronunciations of
Australian English.

I'm still not persuaded that pidgins are a way forward for an
international language.  One problem is that an opportunistic
dialect cannot be systematic.  Perhaps it is not really true that
the grammar is simple, but rather that it snatches phrases from
the parent language, and the possible phrases in that language are
determined by a grammar which may be complex.

Regards,
Michael Talbot-Wilson

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 04 Aug 2000 23:41:20 -0700


> I'm still not persuaded that pidgins are a way forward for an
> international language.  One problem is that an opportunistic
> dialect cannot be systematic.

This is true; a random pidgin/creole needs much more
systemizing before it can serve as a global interlanguage.
Perhaps a better example would be Bahasa Indonesia,
which has served as a regional IAL, though still not
(perhaps) systematic enough for global use. But we should
learn what we can from these two examples; Glosa is
the only well-known IAL with a grammar of this type.

An isolating language *can* be as systematic as desired.
I think Glosa can be improved "merely" by reforming its
vocabulary, whereas other IALs are not so easily reformed,
judging by past history. I do believe that less grammar
is for the best, and there is hope for this approach.
>From what I have seen on other lists, other grammars
(such as Ido) are never fully mastered, and so most
people remain too uncomfortable to *say* anything.
We need something much simpler for a real-world IAL.

But I'd like to hear more about what would constitute
"systematic", specifically. The Ido suffixes are not
so scientific as claimed, and certainly agglutination
is not going to win over the masses of the world.
I have several ideas on this, but nothing definitive.
The key is that vocabulary is flexible, grammar is stiff.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 5 Aug 2000 17:09:48 +0930 (CST)


> >From what I have seen on other lists, other grammars
> (such as Ido) are never fully mastered, and so most
> people remain too uncomfortable to *say* anything.

A somewhat daring claim, since "other grammars" are used
exclusively in their mailing lists (though admittedly, perhaps, by
the enthusiatic few), when that can't be said for Glosa.  Ido, you
say?  No-one writes in English to the Ido list.  If they did, they
would not be understood by several people who freely engage in
discussions there, especially the French and the Russians.

It's probably true of Ido, though, as of anything else, that many
people don't "fully" master it.  But people who have been learning
it for a day *say* things.

> But I'd like to hear more about what would constitute
> "systematic", specifically. The Ido suffixes are not
> so scientific as claimed, and certainly agglutination
> is not going to win over the masses of the world.

Hmm...  Don't know how scientific they are claimed to be.  The
language strikes me as (as it were) a kind of compromise arrived
at by a very gifted committee.  I sense a kind of moderation, as
though principles, good in themselves, had not been pushed to
extremes.  It may well be that it is therefore unscientific.  
Perhaps it is artistic.

However, this is a Glosa list.  What I (instant expert) suggested
about Tok Pisin is that it is undefined, that it perpetually
shadows English.  (In part that is an unfair comparison.  English
is also undefined.  But it has been satisfactorily described, and
the description is autonomous.)

Being systematic, perhaps, means having an autonomous grammar.  
When talking about IALs, being systematic also means that the
grammar is well-defined, and perhaps completely defined.  Even if
it supposedly has no grammar, there is a predefined way, for
example, to signify number.  But that's not totally satisfactory.  
There is a prefined way (with "wanpela") to do that in Tok Pisin.

So don't press me too far on "systematic".  I mean something, or
it means something to me, but I'll leave it at that.

Regards,
Michael Talbot-Wilson

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 5 Aug 2000 18:31:53 +0930 (CST)

> However, this is a Glosa list.  What I (instant expert) suggested
> about Tok Pisin is that it is undefined, that it perpetually
> shadows English.  (In part that is an unfair comparison.  English

What I meant by Tok Pisin (and presumably pidgins in general)
being unsystematic was that, if it takes a phrase from the parent
language opportunistically, the phraseology is not derived from
any system within the language.  

For example, when the Australian media are blathering at length
about (Fiji) "coup leader George Speight", and then we see "ku
lida George Speight" on the Tok Pisin site, does the phrase "ku
lida" conform to the pattern of the language, and did its separate
words even exist in the language (the pidgin) prior to this story
making the news?  I don't claim to certainly know the answer, I'm
just using this example to trigger some guesses.

Could "ku lida" be generated within Tok Pisin, or could it only
arise because that expression became newsworthy in English?  In
the event that an independent Tok Pisin site had been first to
market with the news of the coup d'Ã©tat, could the expression "ku
lida George Speight" have been used by it from the first?

I'm suggesting that if the language is imitative to a high degree,
the phrase does not arise in accordance with any system within it.

>2. add prepositional phrases after nouns
>
>That's enough! Adding more features just makes it worse.
>
*   Very brief, but to the point.  Ron Clark delighted in saying that a
word was modified by its precedent.
    It is possible to find that in both English and Glosa, the story
snowballs from the first word of the sentence to the last.  All along the
line, words in front modify the next, and so on.

>Glosa es ma facili de Esperanto!!
>Glosa nu debi repara u lexi-lista ...
>
*   Both are correct: one involves me in a bit of work.

Saluta,
  Robin

>A set of rules that work both ways from Greek roots to Glosa words and
>back again automatically turns any classical Greek dictionary into a
>Glosa dictionary.

    The Greek alphabet causes me to bog down every time: there is no direct
transliteration between the Roman alphabet and the Greek!

    I'm sure that a small number of 'rules of thumb' could be devised to
source the Greek dictionary for suitable roots to add to the Glosa lexicon,
but I would bet that there is no formula.

    What Ron Clark liked about the Greek lexicon was iits flexibility: the

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Solution to Glosa's Vocab problem
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 05 Aug 2000 20:26:40 +1000

At 23:36 25/07/2000 +0930, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:
>At 1999-07-25 09:20 -0400, Jay Bowks wrote:
>
>> ...
>> The original Interglossa idea was to use a minimum
>> set of roots as in Basic English but derive these from
>> ...
>> I do agree that 1000 words is just bare minimum. And
>> 1200 to 2500 words would be more usable; also the
>> Glosa 6000 would need weeding out and more work
>> done on compounding for the sake of adding terms
>> to the wordlists instead. 
>
>But on the Basic principle, shouldn't it be possible, with that
>bare minimum or whatever is on the Net (Charles says it is 1800 -
>I didn't count them), to hold these discussions in the
>language?  Why are we talking English?
>
*   There is an answer to this question, and it is fairly simple: most of
us speak normal English, and if that was what we used on the Glosalist,
then we could have a majority of news and ideas in Glosa.
    But there IS a 'language about language' and this is what Linguists
speak.  And many on the Glosalist want to talk Linguistically about Glosa:
such discussions do not work in the everyday language of pedestrians.

>Yes, on the principle of word economy, too many means too many to
>learn.  A rule to generate words might not be such a good idea.  
>(Hogben's criticism of Interlingua de Peano -- too many words.)
>
*   Glosa was never meant to be a set piece, and, as a language of science,
it would logically need to accommodate all new concepts, theories and bits
of science .. as they turn up.
    I do, however have a formula for deriving new words for the Glosa
lexicon.  I look u the Langensheidt _Shorter Latin Dictionary_ by feeding
in the English word into the back half, and get a range of likely-looking
Latin words.  From these I mentally shorten the words to suitable lengths
so that they look like Glosa words; then I check out my short-list of the
words in the front of the dictionary to see iif they hold the meaning and
suitable formin the Latin to English section.  When I have narrowed down my
selection, I look in Glosa 6000 to see that there is a linguistic space to
accommodate the new word, IE it doesn't look too close to an existing Glosa
word.  Then I write mu newly coined Glosa word into the "6000".
    Acase in point occurred when I sought the Glosa word for "employment" 
in "6000".  There was no entry covering it, and when I did my search in the
Latin Dictionary, the result, after rounding, was ~utor~.  This word was
close to ~uti~ for "use", but far enough away to be distinctive.

Saluta,
   Robin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: Scooping, Always Scooping
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 05 Aug 2000 21:02:30 +1000

At 16:23 26/07/2000 -0700, Joe Carson wrote:
>Robin wrote: "... if Novial/Ido/Interlingua/Occidental crowd had been able to
>reach consensus mode, they would have ^scooped the pot^ long ago."
>
>Here's a classic case in point of how illustrative idioms can be less
>than transparent among contemporary speakers of the same language, even
>when their basic meanings can be guessed at from the context of their
>usage to make a point. What is the pithy expression "scoop the pot"
>itself derived from? 
>   .....
Joe, I used a gambling term here because I see the various auxlangs  (and
auxlang groups) as sitting around a gambling pit each throwing in their
bids, and each wishing the win the pot of gold that will come with IAL
Selection.

   Back to reality, when gambling on cards, often the stakes in the middle
of the table build up as no-one wins the hands.  This necessitates putting
the money into a container, possibly a "pot" to stop the coins from
spilling out into the playing area.  Hence the use of the term "jack-pot"
to cover the large amount of money that has been steadily building up,
waiting for a winner to "scoop the pot"

   It has long been my understanding that Country Australians enjoy using
colourful language and the telling of tall tales.  It is also a part of the
alure of Australia for me.  

   However, there could be a loss of enjoyment in the act of communication
if the adopted IAL has no scope for idiomatic and metaphorical usages. So,
as the result of a discussion a couple of years ago on the Conlang List, I
decided that we ought to be able to use colourful language in our
international communications, as long as we marked them as such, using a
symbol to denote the use of non-literal language.

   Shortening the story, I now might describe heavy rain, by saying, "It's
raining ^cats and dogs^."

   In the gamble for IAL status, perhaps Glosa will ^scoop the pot.^

Saluta, 
  Robin

At 16:48 26/07/2000 -0700, Charles wrote:
>
>>     With Glosa the Part-of-Speech markers are notably absent,
>>unnecessary, or missing.  Which, I can't be sure; but, up to now, I have
>>been working on the premise that the proper placement of a word in a
sentence was all the
>> signposting its function needed.
>
>Adding u/plu and pa/nu etc particles helps a lot, though
>it can get ugly if overdone. Prepositions are still difficult,
>but all the auxlangs have too many prepositions for my taste.
>
*   Yes, agreed, the use of prepositions in English suggest that I might
use the corresponding Glosa equivalent.  But this woild often be wrong,
English usage is often more learned than logical.     

    Ron Clark declared ~de~ to be the #multipurpose# preposition in Glosa.
So, when you were telling a story, requiring a preposition, butyou didn't
know which, then the odds were that it would be ~de~.  Wher I want to
indicate the 'infinitive, but don't feel that ~te~ is correct, I use ~de~.

At 12:44 25/07/2000 +0930, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:

>Charles wrote:

>

>> 

>> >[RG] *   In what sense do you feel 'offended' by the use of the Glosa >> > abbreviations that form the "generic" terms?

>> >     They represent abbreviated compounds, EG

>> >       pani boteka   [bread shop]

>> >       pani-boteka

>> >       pani-bo       [baker's]

>> > 

>> >    These generics make the language much more usable for me, and they 

>> > slip off the tongue easily, as well.

>> 

>> That's just *morphology*, i.e. suffixes and prefixes

>> in a thin disquise, eliminating Glosa's claim to fame.

>> (I'm not counting word-compounding as morphology.)

>> There is no other well-known creole-like auxlang, that's

>> my interest in Glosa (perhaps Lingua Franca can be revived).

>> 

>> As a set of word-building affixes, the "bo-" set are clearly

>> inferior to those of the other auxlangs, Ido for instance.

>> Far better to drop them and use plain compounds, I think.

>>

>> participles or some such useful things. But that would

>

*  Ah so, Michael vo habe diskursi uti Glosa: ci, id es.

   Ka an habe multi qestio, mi ne habe tem te dona plu England-lingua eqa-ra.

   Supra u-ci, Charles grafo de verbi-morfo, plu bi-litera fixa-ra, e u natura de un uti-abili de u ge-tekto "kreole" lingua.

   Pre nu, mi pa grafo de plu Glosa partikula; dice ke mu usuali habe separa eso, e so, eqa plu separa verbi.  Kron na vo uti u verbi-radika te deskribe u difere kategori de ra, ko u difere semani, na ne crea u flexi-

verbi, na forma u neo komplexi-verbi.  Tem "runner" (~kurso-pe~) posi eqa u ge-flexi verbi pro Charles e Michael, mi ne vide id so; habe u klari define-ra de ~flexi-ra~ iso plu Akti-Verbi; qi habe mo, alo ma, muta, te modifi mu akti.

   Mi logi ke u 'kreole' eqa u separa lingua, in qi ni de plu kontribu lingua grega loka mu plu idioma.  U funktio de un origi #pidgin# lingua es pro komerci, e u resulta lingua habe fo direkti expresi.  U diificili ra es tem: po plu komerci-pe produce u gru lingua inter mu bi difere lingua traditio, plu persona; qi uti u-ci hibridi lingua, game e produce plu ju-pe.  U direkti; in qi u neo lingua ki, pende de u ge-relatio influ ex plu bi kutura; qi pa forma u pidgin.  

   So, mi kredi il ne eqa ali perfekti kreole intra histori.  Singu kaso habe difere.  Anti-co, usuali plu navi de u visita kultura ki ab, e plu ju-pe; qi crea u kreole, habe bio intra u lokali kultura.  Nuli de plu idioma ex plu-pe de u navi-ge-fero kultura fu es tena intra u kreole, ka plu visita komerci-pe sio es longi-tem ki ex la.


   Per qo-mode uno-pe face u simi-kreole lingua?  Mu sedi, e piktura plu krudi pirati-pe ex u previo tem.  Seqe-co, mu puta de plu ge-civiliza uti de lingua; qi plu pirati-pe sio ne uti.  Po seqe u-ci line de kogita, mu ariva to u fo funktio basi lingua.  E po-co, mu adi plura de plu lingua qalita; qi mu imagina de es intra un origi lingua de plu lokali-pe.

   Po holo ci, u persona; qi vo crea u gru simi-kreole lingua, concentra an imagine epi plu prima e sekunda genera de homi; dice u neo lingua, e qestio-auto: qod ra plu enfanta, e plu enfanta enfanta, fu dice de.  mu fu qestio-auto: in qi direkti fu kresce u neo hibridi kultura; e per qo mode plu neo genera de kreole-dice-pe fu adi a mu lingua.


>This isn't an attempt to knock Glosa.  I'm just trying to clutch

>at the spirit of it, grasp what it is all about, see if there is

>something in there that is really the way forward.  It is a later

>invention than Ido: is it based on a fundamentally better

>approach, and does it show?

>

*   U spiritu de Glosa es aperi komunika.

    Imagina u lingua no-kumu ko lingua-teori, habe o fo lati ge-distribu lexiko, e ko u fo klari faski de sintaxi regula: Ron Clark pa akti so. 

U resulta pa eqa Glosa. 

    U fo mega dificili ko Glosa es, te uti id, uno-pe nece kogita de plu idea an, alo fe, tende de komunika.  U-ci problem ne es solo mo de traduce; Glosa kausa na de qestio na akti de kogita.  Ko Glosa; ge-designa iso un aperi lingua, te uti u libera id don a na, na nece puta ko klari, e utor plu lingua abili; qi na posi sio ne uti intra na norma, nati-lingua komunika.

    

>Is it, for example, easier to learn?  Are creoles and pigins

>really easy to learn, or are they just an unsophisticated

>compromise?  Do they work well, or do they just allow people to

>get by after a fashion?  Are they just where you finish up when

>improvising across a language barrier?  If so, does that fact mean

>they are easy, or just minimal?  Is minimal good, or just limited?

>

*   Veri, Glosa habe plura minima mode de expresi.  Si u grega de intelige persona, ex vario kultura, nece dice alelo, koncerne u fo importa topika, mu pote gene u skema de Glosa durante mo di, praxi uti ^kapita finali^ syntaxi ["head final" phrase structure] durante u sekunda di, gene akorda koncerne u communi lexiko de plu tekno verbi; qi ne es intra u Glosa diktionari, durante di tri; e, seqe-co, komence uti u lingua iso u direkti komununika media.


>Is Chinese really easy to learn? Issues relating to Chinese may

>include (a) there are too many words; (b) grammatical

>simplification was accompanied by great elaboration of idiomatic

>phraseology.  Has such a set of helper phrases (presumably a few

>thousand of them) been provided for Glosa?

>

*   U Cina lingua, iso holo plu natio-lingua, habe multi speciali uti; qi pa gene inklude intra u-la lingua, tem plu cent-anua e kilo-anua, de id  existe.

    Plu Glosa autorii-pe pa evita crea plu idioma intra Glosa, dice ke id nece habe nuli idioma pro munda uti.  Mi puta: u-ci idea ne habe ero.  Sed, id es u pos-abili, ke u maxi auxi, pro u disemina de Glosa, sio fu-pa es u produce de u bibli de Glosa idioma.


>On ease of learning of Glosa, the most conspicuous piece of

>evidence that I have discovered is that the discussions in this

>simply a FAQ in English posted here regularly, and the actual

*   Exakti so.  Plu kogita-pe de u Glosalist, prefero de kogita de Glosa e ne prefero de kogita in id.  U lingua ne gene toto ge-kompleti ex Ron pre an morta, e u-ci faktu kausa na de mira id finali forma, vice praxi id, e grafo plu bibli uti id.

   Mi vo negotia u tali finali forma: alo u puri 6000+ plus u boni sati 2500; alo u Glosa 3.  Sed mi habe pusi tem pro un ergo, e mi ne ski plu puta de Wendy Ashby.  Mi kredi ke u Glosa 2500 diktionari debi sio es mi fu-ergo.


>Does it take 60 years to invent or instinctively create a pidgin

*   Tu plu numera ne habe justi: ex 1943 a 1972 u proto-lingua "Interglossa" pa sed intra id bibli.

    Ron Clark pa uti u demi dek-anua tenta uti u Hogben skema - 1972-1977; 

    An pa uti u sekunda demi dek-anua te simplifi u skema e produce u prima verbi-lista - 1978-1983;

    U plus dek-anua pa gene ge-uti face poli pusi muta, te produce u finali forma, plus u ge-ple 6000-verbi diktionari - 1984-1992;

    Durante u maxi mero de u tri dek-anua, plu autori-pe e mi pa tenta promoti u lingua dia u munda - 1993-2000.


   Pre tu aloka etio ad u Glosa grega, mi sugere: tu skope u nu munda.

Tem u developo de Esperanto e plu hetero auxi-lingua, plu-pe ne pa habe televidio e plu puta-me te turba mu.  Mi don apologi pro u munda!

   Pro reali komunika - zero.

   Il nuli-tem pa es u Glosa Konfere!

   Itera, mi don apologi pro u munda.

   Posi na fu gene u ma boni responde epi u planeta, Mars.


>One of Hogben's key insights, or so he thought, concerned the

>importance of word economy, as exemplified by Basic English.  The

>thing that takes so long in learning a language is acquiring the

>words.  The only criticism he has of Peano's Interlingua was that

*   Ron Clark pa dice id, ko u pusi difere: an pa dice ke 1000 verbi de Glosa eqa proxi 3000 verbi de ali hetero lingua.  An pa dice: ka plu poli uti de Plu Glosa verbi, mu pa habe mega ma dina.

    Pro tekno uti, Glosa nece habe poli ge-asocia, adi speciali-subjekti lexiko.  U-ci ergo, a-nu, ne gene ge-proto.  So, Glosa pa gene ge-sto, demi-ge-kompleti.


>It should therefore be possible to carry on written discussions in

*   Konversa, uti solo u prima kilo Glosa verbi es ge-limita.

    Ka mi veni ad Australia, ex England, pre Glosa 6000 gene ge-kompleti, mi ne habe un oportuni de dice ko Ron e Wendy uti u-ci ple lexiko.

    Ko-co, mi pa tenta uti u mo kilo lista te grafo plu grama, e pa detekti u dificili intra mi plu frasa. Un absenti de plu simi-nyma pa kausa mi grafo es plato.  Sine u vario de plu alterno forma, mi pa este ke mi grafo ne pa habe kroma.

    Kron grafo u-ci e-posta, mi du uti u verti-ge-kroma _Glosa 6000_ diktionari.  Gene u kopi de u-ci bibli sio fo auxi tu uti de u lingua.


>I have tried as a rank beginner, with rather little success.  I

>could only guess and hope that the accumulation of words was

>intelligible. 

*   Grafo glosa ne pende de spe; id pende de u korekti uti de sintaxi.


>   It seems that because there is little formal

>grammar there is no formal sentence.  

*   In ero, itera.  U frasa forma veni ex un aplika de plu regula de Minor Grega [Phrase] ordina; e ex u Kapita Finali stru intra plu Minor Grega.

    SUBJEKTI [NP]           AKTI-VERBI [VP]        OBJEKTI [NP]

u-numera-modifi-nima^verbi  tem-modifi-akti^verbi  u- ....... -nima-verbi

EG

Plu penta itelige Lingua-pe fu du fo-sono demanda u ge-typo gramatika.

[the five intellig. Linguists will be loudly asking for a printed grammar]

...................._________................___....___..........._______    

  

>       There is nothing you can

>analyse and determine to be a complete sentence, and therefore a

>verifiably complete and verifiably intelligible communication of a

>thought.  Whereas a rank beginner in Ido has no difficulty in

>creating a sentence.

>

*   Mi evita u tende de dice, remane ko Ido.

    Vice, mi qestio tu Glosa textu!?  Place lekto plu ge-pende semina info.

 

>And that language is the normal medium of communication among

>students of it.  And the root+suffix system is a reasonable

>response to the word-economy issue.  That's why Zamenhof adopted

>it in Esperanto.

>

*   Ja, u radika+fixa sistema funktio intra Ido; e u radika & sintaxi sistema funktio intra Glosa.

    Hogben e Clark simpli pa greso in u direkti; opositi ex Zamenhof.


>On ease of use and ease of learning, Glosa does not even seem to

>be in the game.  

*   No.  Te gene sko de Glosa, uno-pe semble nece uti u mero de eforti.

    Alterno, mi pa grafo u Gramatika Tabla pro Glosa.  Si tu prefere u regula-ge-basi metodi de stude, tu posi fu progresi ma celero per lekto plu gramatika regula, e aplika mu.  Bali a mi un e-posta si tu vo gene un IBM disko ko plu 6000 lista (E->G e G->E) e u kopi de u Gramatika.


Therefore, what are we doing here?  Is it just a

>matter of loyalty, of irrational attachment?

>

*   Plura-tem mi habe u homo qestio.

    Si ma persona dona ma eforti a Glosa id fu vola.  Recento, u numera de persona pa jugo u grega, e, boni fortuna, pa gene u boni resulta ko u lingua.

    Mi habe pistio pro homi: si plu persona de u-ci planeta posi fu dice alelo, e mi logi ke Glosa es u fru metodi te gene u-ci, mi posi pluso habe pistio pro Glosa.

    Mi bio fu es ma simpli sine Glosa.  Sed na bio posi sio ofere ma de 

gluko existe.  


>I've looked around on the Internet trying to find what is the key

>advantage of Glosa relative to other IALs.  As I understand it,

>the key claim is that it has no inflexions, genders, "or

>diacritics" (!).  And that it has special words (rather than

>inflexions) to signify the plural.  That is the key claim, and

>presumably is supposed to be the key advantage.  Is it an

>advantage?  We have seen (and we are seeing above) that Glosa has

>proceded to acquire inflexions.  Interlingua also acquired

>grammar.

>

*   U-ci experi monstro ke un Inter-reti ne habe holo-ra.

    Te logi Glosa tu posi nece gene o papira dictionari.  Pluso, tu pote gene info ex un IBM disko; nuli epi u Reti.

    Flexi-verbi??

  ~ge-grafo~ -  pote gene ge-dice es u flexi-verbi: solo mo intra u holo lingua. 

    Intra tu lista de Glosa qalita, mi este; tu pa lose un eso de Glosa.

    Posi tu ne hedo sintaxi.  Mi fu inklude ko plu pende-ra u skema in qi mi pa extrakti plu regula de sintaxi: imagina plu elementa de sintaxi sine plu verbi!  Mi pa uti plu ASCII simboli te monstro plu mero de sintaxi intra u "Gas" fila.


>Glosa seems to be a language that after nearly 60 years of

>development, four times as long as any other IAL, has not reached

>the point of being usable, and is not used.  Is this a fair

>assessment, or am I being unreasonable, or clueless?

>

*   Tu pote tenta uti ma imagina.

    Sine u Glosa 6000 dictionari, u gramatika tabla, e u logi de syntaxi, tu pote sio es minus plu indika.


>I can understand that it might be of interest to students of

>language, of linguistics, as an object of experiment.  Is this the

>real purpose of Glosa?

>

*   No!

    Id es u lingua pro science.

    Pluso, id gene ge projecti iso u lingua pro holo homi.


>A toy for linguists I can understand and sympathise with.  Of

>course, that would be a specialist tool that the rest of us would

>be ill-advised to waste our time with.

>

*   Penite, no!

    Mi habe pusi simpati ko plu Lingua-stude-pe.

    Mi habe simpati ko plu tekno-stude-pe: mu solve plu problema, e gene plu resulta; plu Lingua-stude-pe, alterno, akti ni de plu-ci ra.


Saluta,

  Robin

_A first Seminar on Glosa____________________________R. Gaskell Sept,1995_
               (With apologies, in English)
WORDS
        Words in Glosa represent concepts, and are really `concept/words.'
They represent ideas, but, depending on their position in the sentence,
and their relationships to other words, they may represent that idea in its
solid form (noun), active form (verb), or as a modifier of another idea
(adjective, adverb).

        The words do not change for reasons of grammar (system for getting
understanding from words): this means that words can be used, within
reason, as any part of speech, but have no letters added to indicate this
useage (no Part-of-Speech markers); also, the words which act as verbs,
`verbs', have no endings added to them (no inflections) to give added
understanding of how, or when, the action is taken.

        The order of the words (syntax) gives us the meaning in Glosa; the
language can be described as having `syntax-based grammar.'

PHRASES
        The phrase (small group of words) is the basic unit of sentence
construction in Glosa.  A small group of words might include a thing and
some description of it (noun phrase), or the group might include an action
and some modifications to this action (verb phrase).  A small group of
words at the start of a Glosa sentence might qualify the action of the
sentence (adverbial phrase).

        Within each phrase, each word is modified by the one before it, and
there is a gradual increase in importance of the words, with the main
concept/word (`noun' or `verb') as the last word of the phrase.
                e.g.  the three fast, loud red cars                  NP
                      plu tri celero fo-sono rubi vagona

                e.g.  were quickly end excitedly talking loudly      VP
                      pa du celero e excita fo-sono dice

                e.g.  while running     (relates to  --->  verb)     AdvP
                      tem kursi

        In speech, phrases are said as one group, often with a raising then
lowering of intonation (pitch), from start to finish; in written form, if
it seems necessary for the understanding of meaning, the start and finish
of phrases, is indicated using punctuation (commas).

CLAUSES
        A clause is a larger group of words containing two (NP + VP) or
more phrases, and such a simple two or three phrase clause can make up the
whole sentence (major clause).  However, a clause - which always includes
at least one verb phrase - might add meaning to the action of the
sentence (Adverbial Clause), might give more description to one of the
things in a sentence (Adjectival Clause), or it could be a group of words,
including a `verb,' used in the place that you would normally find a noun
phrase (Noun Clause).

      e.g.  The boy arrived.              U ju-an pa ariva.            MC

      e.g.  The girl went home.           U ju-fe pa ki a fe domi.     MC

      e.g.  The man will eat the meal.    Un andra fu vora u vora.     MC

      e.g.  She spoke so loudly that she was heard.
            Fe pa ta sono dice ke fe pa gene ge-audi.                AdvC
                          ~~~~ <-- ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

      e.g.  The girl went home after she had eaten a good meal.
            U ju-fe pa ki a fe domi, po fe pra vora u boni vora.     AdvC
                       ~~   <---     ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  The boy, who was fat and badly dressed, arrived.
            U ju-an; qi pa es paki e mali ge-vesti, pa ariva.        AdjC
              ~~~~~ <-- ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  I like to get away from the house.
                  (getting)
            Mi amo gene ab u domi.                                     NC
                   ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  The farmer hoped that it would rain.
            Un agri-pe pa spera: ke id sio pluvi.                      NC
                       ~~~(V)~~  ~~~~(O)~~~~~~~~

SEQUENCE IN A CLAUSE
        The normal sequence in Glosa is the same as in English:-
 Main Clause =   noun phrase    -    verb phrase     -    noun phrase
                  (SUBJECT)            (VERB)              (OBJECT)
            Doer of the action    Action being done    Thing acted upon
           e.g.     He                 posts               a letter.
                    An                 posta               u grama.

        There are three main variations to this: clauses with no object
(intransitive verb); clauses where, for reasons of style, the speaker,
or writer, wishes to emphasise the object by placing it first (Passive
Voice); and Noun Clauses which have no subject (infinitive verb).
           e.g.    She (NP)    sang. (VP)                 Fe pa kanta.
                 (SUBJECT)    (VERB)

           e.g.   The letter       was posted        by the man.
                   U grama      pa gene ge-posta     ex un andra.
                               (did get got-post)
                   (OBJECT)         (VERB)                (SUBJECT)

           e.g.                  - running (=to run)     on wet grass
                                    kursi              epi ge-hidra gra
                                    (VERB)                 (OBJECT)

        Because its grammar depends on the word order, Glosa works best if
we keep to the normal (S-V-O) structure in clauses.  In poetry and fiction,
however, the stylistic variations are necessary.

FUNCTION WORDS
        These are the small words that are not concept/words, but which
join these words together (conjunctions), lead us into a phrase or clause
(prepositions), create negatives, introduce questions, or modify the timing
of an action when placed before a `verb' (tense particles).  There are also
the small words placed before `nouns' to tell us that they are things, and
how many, or how much, of them there are (determinants).
        Joining words:  e, sed, pluso, alo, ni

        Prepositions: tem, a, ex, de, anti, seqe, po, pre, vice, krom ....

        Negatives: ne, nuli, no-, ni ... ni, nuli-

        Questions: Qe, qestio, qod, qo-

        Tense particles: pa, fu, du, nu, pra, sio, ge-, gene

        Before `nouns': u, plu, plura, uno, vario, poli, oligo, u mero de
         (determinant)

SENTENCES
        Putting all the above together, in Glosa, as in English, sentences
can be made up of various combinations of phrases and clauses - as long as
the Subject-Verb-Object rule is observed.  In Glosa, also, there is the
added requirement that words in phrases follow the rule of increasing
importance, with the main concept/word last.

        If the sentence is made up of phrases only, there is a single
`verb', and the sentence is described as a Major Clause.  If there are two
or more `verbs', then the `verb' describing the main action forms the Main
Clause, and the other clauses (NC, AdvC, AdjC) are called Subordinate
Clauses.  The whole sentence can, however, still be called the Major
Clause.
        The secret of good sentence formation is in using well-formed
phrases to make up the sentences and their constituent clauses.

PRECEDENCE IN PHRASES
RULE: In a phrase, a word is modified by the word in front of it (its
        precedent.)
RULE: In a normal sentence (Major Clause), there is a tendency for the
        first noun phrase (SUBJECT) to modify the verb phrase (VERB), and
        then, for the verb phrase to modify the second noun phrase (OBJECT).
RULE: A modifying clause (Adjectival or Adverbial) follows directly after
        the concept/word that it modifies; this structure is indicated
        by the placing of a semi-colon (;) or possibly a colon (:) before
        the new clause.

 In a noun phrase, the sequence is:-

           determinant  -  number  -  quantity  -  modifier(s)  -  noun

        e.g. plu tri no-ge-numera, fo sono, no-puri ju-an
             the three uncounted, shouting, dirty boys

           Note.  The concept/word used as a noun is the only element of
              a noun phrase that is essential.

 In a verb phrase, the sequence is:-
                                                    |   verb
  negative - tense - modifier(s) - auxiliary verb - |      OR
 particle                                           | verboid + amplifier

                     e.g. ne pa hedo, no-soni tenta | kursi
                                                    | ki ana

                       did not happily, quietly try | to run
                                                    | to go up

           Note, again.  The concept/word used as a verb is the only
               element of a verb phrase that is essential.

 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

_A second Seminar on Glosa____________________________R. Gaskell Sept,1998_
-----------------------------______________________------- File: SEMINAR.#2

BASIC GRAMMAR RULES
                     There are three basic rules behind the grammar of
Glosa: combined, they tell us 'position determines function'.

        1. S-V-O: Subject, Verb, Object order in a clause.
        2. "A word is modified by its precedent."  One word modifies the
             next, and so on.
        3. A word can function, within reason, as any part of speech.
             There are no Part-of-Speech markers, but a word's function is
             indicated by its position in a sentence.
        __________________________________________________________________
        All other rules of Glosa usage follow from these.
All sentences contain a 'verb': it is the word that tells us the action
occurring in the sentence; it gives the sentence _life_.

VERBS
        In Glosa, the word in the middle of the sentence, describing the
action, is the 'verb' ('verb' = word functioning as a verb).

  EG  U kani bibe u hidra.  ...................  bibe  'verb'
             ~~~~
   [The dog drinks the water.]

  EG  An fo hedo bibe.  .......................  hedo  'verb'
            ~~~~   ............................  bibe  'gerund' (verb/noun)
   [He very-much enjoys to drink/drinking.]

  EG  Mi don ad un equs id bibe.  .............  don(a)  'verb'
         ~~~        ...........................  bibe  'noun'
   [I give (to) the horse its drink.]

N.B. 'verb' =  word functioning as a verb
     'noun' =  word functioning as a noun
      ' X ' =  word functioning as an  X

"IS" IN ENGLISH
                 This word is used in many different ways in English, but,
in Glosa, the "is" concept is conveyed more accurately - using a range of
'verbs'.  As an 'action' its original function was to represent an "equals"
sign, EG "He is John."  Glosa translations for various uses of "is" in
English are:-

  es  -  to be, is (has equivalence with), has the essence of
  habe - has the property (quality) of
  gene - gets to be, becomes
  eqa - equals, measures

Examples:
    Fe es u ju-fe.
   [She is a girl.]
    An es u filosofi-pe.
   [He is a philosopher.]

    U meteo habe frigo.
   [The weather is cold/has coldness] [It is cold.]
    Mi habe fo lati.
   [I am very tall/have much tallness.]

    U meteo gene ma termo.
   [The weather is warmer/gets warmer/becomes more hot.]
    U ju-an gene fatiga.
   [The boy is tired/becomes tired.]
    Mi habe fo turba; e fu gene koleri, si tu ne sto dice.
   [I am very upset, and will be angry if you don't stop talking.]

    U temperatura eqa tri-ze grada (30`C).
   [The temperature is thirty degrees (30`C).]
    U preci de ki-in u teatra eqa mo-penta dolara ($15).
   [The price of entry into the theatre is fifteen dollars ($15).]

COMMON 'VERBS'
                In Glosa, some words are used almost exclusively as verbs,
and these are general-purpose verbs, such as:-

  habe - to have, have has
  ki  -  to go, go, goes, went
  veni - to come, come, comes, came
  tena - to hold, grab, take

VERBOIDS
          Some words, in Glosa, are "almost verbs" but are not "full verbs"
in their own right: they are not 'stand-alone' verbs, but they add to the
meaning of the "full verb", which is usually the final word in the Verb
Phrase.  These Verboids are half-way between Adverbs and Verbs.  Examples:-

  posi - possibly
  pote - can, has the ability to
  nece - needs to, must
  lice - be allowed, allow to
  debi - ought to
  hedo - enjoys, happily
  forti - strongly
  seqe - following, sebsequently
  vo(lu) - wish to, would
  tanto - so much, almost
  tosto - soon

EG  Mi fu posi ki ad u boteka.
   [I will possibly go to the shop.]
    An pote kurso fo celero.
   [He can run very quickly.]
    Kron mi voka, fe tosto veni in u domi.
   [When I call, she soon comes into the house.]
    Mi gina fu hedo kanta pro vi.
   [My wife will happily sing for you.]
    Tu lice habe id.
   [You are allowed to have it.]
    Pa noktu, mi tanto ariva pre u pluvi.  .. (Pastness implied by "Pa" so
   [Last night I almost arrived before the rain.]  'pa tanto ariva' wrong.)

AUXILIARY VERBS
                 These words 'help' the verb to do its job.  But they
usually follow the "main verb" as separate words, though they contribute to
the formation of a "compound verb."  Examples are:-

  ana - up   EG  ki ana  [ascend]
  kata - down   EG  ki kata  [descend]
  retro - back   EG  kurso retro  [run back]
  itera - again   EG  akti itera  [do (it) again; repeat]
  ab  -  away   EG  greso ab  [walk away]
  versi - back to start   EG  viagia versi  [travel home]
  avanti - forward   EG  ki avanti  [advance]

EG  An pa ki kata un orto-lo; sed an nece skende itera ana id, te
   [He fell down the cliff, but (he) must climb again up it, in order to

    gene an kefa-ve; qi veni ab an kefa pre an pa kade.
    get his hat, which came off his head before he fell.]

WAYS IN WHICH THE 'VERB' MAY BE MODIFIED
  In Glosa, the word functioning as the verb can be modified by placing
short words, called particles, before it.  These particles might alter the
time of the event, negate it, turn it back on the Subject, or make it
conditional on some other event.  As well as that, the action might act
upon - or not act upon - an Object; and then, for a change, the
positioning Subject and Object is sometimes switched around.

. TENSE PARTICLES
                   Dealt with, in detail - in the Tense Table - these short
words, which come at the start of a Verb Phrase, usually singly but
sometimes in a group, tell us WHEN an action occurs.

     - No particle before the 'verb' suggest a present tense (but can mean
        that an earlier time indication logically applies).
 pa  -  Simple Past Tense  (pa  [did])
 fu  -  Future Tense  (fu  [will])
 nu  -  An immediate Present Tense  (nu  [just now])
 du  -  Continuous Tense   (du X  [is X-ing, continues to X])
 pra -  Past Perfect Tense: fully completed.   (pra  [had])

  EG  Un andra nu pa veni in u domi.
               ~~~~~
     [The man just now came into the house.]

CONDITIONAL
             When an action happens only if something else occurs before
it, then we say that the second event is conditional on the first: in
English, this is usually shown by the use of "would", "should" or "could".

 sio  -  would
 sio debi - would ought, should
 sio posi - would possibly, could

  EG  Si mu gene pluvi, plu-ci sperma sio posi kresce in alti fito.
     [If they get rain, these plants could grow into tall bushes.]

NEGATIVE
          When an action does NOT happen, we indicate this using a negative
word before the verb.  This happens in English, and something very similar
occurs in Glosa.

 ne  -  does not
 no  -  un-
 nuli - never  (also used with 'nouns':  nuli ra  [no thing])
 ni X ni Y - neither X nor Y

  EG  U gina ne ki a plu boteka; ka fe vagona ne ergo.
     [The woman did not go to the shops, because her car did not work.]

REFLEXIVE
            When an action affects the person or thing that caused it, we
say that the action is a "reflexive" action.  In Glosa there are two
particles that are used after the 'verb' to indicate that the action is
received by the Subject of the sentence: one if the Subject is human, the
other for non-human Subjects:-

 -se  -  to itself, itself
 auto -  to himself/herself, himself/herself, themselves

  EG  Jon vagona frakto-se, tem an du age ad an ergo-lo.
     [Jon's car broke itself while he was driving to work (his work-place).]

      Kron fe skende kata plu grada; fe pa kade, e noku auto.
     [When she descended the stairs, she fell and hurt herself.]

TRANSITIVE/
        /INTRANSITIVE   While the standard English sentence, has two Noun
Phrases separated by a Verb Phrase, some sentences have no real Object -
receiver of the action: these are called "intransitive" sentences. Glosa
also follows this pattern:-

  Transitive  S-V-O   EG  U kani fago u karni.  [The dog eats the meat.]
  Intransitive  S-V   EG  U kani voka.    [the dog barks.]
  Intransitive  S-V(-IO)  EG   U kani voka ad u posta-pe.
   IO --> Indirect Object     [The dog barks at the post-man.]

PASSIVE
 VOICE   While most of English and Glosa has the normal structure where
         the thing doing the action comes first, and whatever receives the
action comes last (called Active Voice), both languages allow the reciever
of the action to get a mention at the start of the sentence (called Passive
Voice).  It can be thought of as an (O-) V (-S) sentence:-

 X V Y  -  X does action V to Y   EG  U kani morda un andra.
  .................................  [The dog bites the man.]

 Y gets V-ed by X  -  Y has action V done to it by X
  EG  Un andra gene ge-morda ex u kani.
     [The man gets bitten by the dog.]

 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

--=====================_965446320==_
Content-Type: text/plain; charset="us-ascii"
Content-Disposition: attachment; filename="Gas.!rg"

 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

                     G-ASCII Analysed Syntax (GAS)        File:syntanal.gas
                     -----------------------------             R. Gaskell
            <A system for analysing sentences written in a       Oct `95
                language that uses Syntax-based Grammar>

Rationale
        Perhaps it is possible to analyse the structure of a sentence by
looking at the function of the words - but replacing the words with symbols
that represent their function, to allow us to perceive this structure in
its abstract form.
        Since languages whose grammar is based on word order, and in which
the words do not change according to their function .. where "verbs" are not
inflected, and parts of speech are not marked .. there is a case for finding
a way of noting this sequence of word-functions.  Glosa is a language with
this form of grammar, which can be described as "Syntax-based."
N.B. English grammar is a hybrid, half of which is syntax-based, e.g. _Dog
bites man._ _Man bites dog._
        In analysing the style of usage for a language that has syntax-
based grammar, superior usage styles will demonstrate particular syntactic
patterns: the replacement of words with abstract symbols will make these
patterns much more readily recognised.
        When teaching the use of a language that has syntax-based grammar,
a system of symbols that represent the syntactic functions will allow
instruction at the abstract level.  Whereas children absorb grammar
intuitively, and build their own mental grammar subconsciously, this method
is more suitable for adult learners, who have usually lost the mental
plasticity they had when children, and who rely for their learning on
conscious, intellectual processes.  In short, adults learn through applying
principles, while children use the natural, unconscious method.  A system,
that allowed adults to perceive the principles of good syntax-based usage,
seemed worth developing.

An Unwritten Grammar
        Glosa is one of the few fully-formed languages that is taught
without the aid of a grammar book.
        The authors, Ronald Clark and Wendy Ashby, have avoided attempting
to tabulate the grammar of Glosa, suggesting that the language relies on
our inherent linguistic ability, and that a "formal" grammar would restrict
the use of the language.  This seems to be true; there is also the thought
that writing a grammar to describe the language of thought would be
difficult, if not impossible.
        People, who have retained some of their child-like flexibility of
mind, can intuit the grammar of Glosa, building a mental grammar for it, in
their heads.  Children learn Glosa very readily, because with its standard-
ised syntax there are few rules of usage, and these can be absorbed easily.

Syntax in the Abstract
        Although not wishing to try to write a "Gramatika," I thought an
abstract representation of Glosa syntax would be in order.  Then I started
looking around for suitable symbols to represent these syntactic functions.
While casually looking at a book on meaning representation, I noticed that
it was full of pseudo-algebraic formulations: I realised that most of these
symbols would not transmit through the Internet as e-mail; it would be
silly to use such symbols for a syntactic analysis, so I looked for
something simpler.
        On looking down at the keyboard of my computer, I found the answer:
I should obviously use ASCII symbols!  So, GAS was born - G-ASCII Analysed
Syntax: I started out awarding a different non-alphanumeric symbol to each
new syntactic function I found as I analysed Glosa text.  But, this way, I
was soon to run out of keyboard symbols.
        Becoming systematic, I wondered how many syntactic functions there
were: this was reminiscent of an earlier question about the number of types
of verbs and nouns.  We would need a rational number of syntactic
categories: not so many it was unworkable; and not so few it was meaning-
less.  Then I started to count the symbols on my 101 key keyboard: there
were the normal 26 letters, each with upper and lower case versions; then
there were the 10 numbers; and after that, I counted 32 non-alphanumeric
symbols.

        I decided it would be nice if the syntactic categories could be
restricted to this last group of 32 symbols, so they wouldn't get mixed up
with the normal text.  But, already, I had got up to 38 syntactic
categories, each of which had been awarded a symbol, plus I had another
seven categories represented as two-symbol compounds: this yielded a total of
45 syntactic categories.  I wondered if this was too many, and if I would
have to slim it down a bit.

GAS
        Here is the first attempt to put it all together:-

            The G-ASCII Analysed Syntax (GAS) system  (Alpha Test Ver.)
            ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

Basic        .          !          >           @           $

        substantive   action   modifier   space, time   logical
          (noun)      (verb)  (adjective,  preposition   preposition
                                adverb)


Tense        /          \          ~           ^           |

          future      past     continuous  conditional    now



Modifiers    #          %          >           v

          number     quantity   quality    auxiliary
       (countable) (measurable) (property)   verb


Conjunctions            +                    &

              joins words, phrases     structural: joins clauses


Functions     x         t          =           <           <`

          location    time    equals, like   verb is    participle
       X proper noun          as , similar   passive


              0         ?          -           ,           ;

       negative: un-   general   joining    pronoun    pronoun
     no, not, never   question   concepts   personal  impersonal
      nothing                   (compounds)


People         o             s              '              `.

            other          self        possessive        gerund
        O proper noun   S name of
                         1st person


Specific       ?o        ?.        ?!         ?x          ?t
 questions
              who       what       why      where        when


Clauses    (      )       {       }       [       ]      "      "

          adjectival        noun          adverbial     parenthesis
                                                        or quotation

Non-literal          :            *       *         _         _
 language
              metaphor or           idiom          start     end
              other n-l term                        of sentence


Syntactic    no space          two spaces       three spaces
 space      within a phrase  between function    between
                             word and phrase,      phrases
                             clause boundary


Not shown:-  determinants    plurality
              (a, the)         ( -s)

>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>><<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<

Examples of GAS in application

1.  The cat sat on the mat.    =     U felis pa sed epi u tape.

        _.   \!   @._


2.  While three fat boys sat by the river bank, and ate jam sandwiches,
     their sisters stole their bicycles.

    _[t  #>o   \!   @.-.   &   \!   >.]  ,'o   \!   ,'._

  Tem tri paki ju-an pa sed proxi u ripa, e pa fago plu konfekti pani,
     mu plu fe-sibi pa klepto mu plu bi-rota.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 05 Aug 2000 10:13:44 -0700


> A somewhat daring claim, since "other grammars" are used
> exclusively in their mailing lists

I was on some Ido lists for a year or two, and though
the people (those visible) did use Ido, the level of
attainment was poor in all but 2 or 3 cases. I don't
intend to blame either the people or the language,
both were admirable, but this (Ido) isn't sufficiently
easy to serve as a truly global auxiliary language.
Even among Europeans the failure rate is too high,
how much worse among non-Euros? So I turned to the
more simplified grammar of Glosa, and found that it has
too many sharp edges about it, but that it should be
easy to reform, by a careful selection of vocabulary.

> It's probably true of Ido, though, as of anything else, that many
> people don't "fully" master it.  But people who have been learning
> it for a day *say* things.

Yes, and Ido was a giant step forward. I am convinced though
(and so was Otto Jesperson) that Occidental went another
giant step forward beyond Ido, keeping its strengths and
reducing its weaknesses. However, I am thinking that perhaps
OCC is best seen as a regional IAL, for those who already
speak a European language. Current research seems to suggest
that only an analytic/isolating language can ever hope to be
an effective global (not regional) auxiliary. My criterion
is that 90% of the adults who attempt to learn it,
must *succeed* (rather than 90% failure as it is today).

> > But I'd like to hear more about what would constitute
> > "systematic", specifically.

> Hmm...  Don't know how scientific they are claimed to be.  The
> language strikes me as (as it were) a kind of compromise arrived
> at by a very gifted committee.  I sense a kind of moderation, as
> though principles, good in themselves, had not been pushed to
> extremes.  It may well be that it is therefore unscientific.
> Perhaps it is artistic.

Ido made heavy claims in this area, and these were IMO fully
*justified* at the time (1907 through 1910). The leading
theorist was Couturat, a mathematician of perhaps questionable
character, but other respected scientists such as Jesperson
played important roles. There is a wealth of information
available in James Chandler's web pages. But it was clear
to most interlinguists in the 1920's that Occidental was
far more advanced, so Ido lost most of its adherents.

> However, this is a Glosa list.  What I (instant expert) suggested
> about Tok Pisin is that it is undefined, that it perpetually
> shadows English.  (In part that is an unfair comparison.

It is partly unfair, but the issue is really Glosa not TP.
You gave a good example about "ku lida"; that sort of thing
must be avoided, agreed. Glosa can be more systematic than that.

> Being systematic, perhaps, means having an autonomous grammar.
> When talking about IALs, being systematic also means that the
> grammar is well-defined, and perhaps completely defined.

As with Beaufront's Ido grammar. OK, I agree that Glosa needs
better definition than it has today. But people familiar with
creoles already know this pattern, which is extremely simple,
and maximally so. Creoles have been unfairly reviled; they're
as expressive and precise as any other human languages
(what they now term "pidgins" are something less than that).

> So don't press me too far on "systematic".  I mean something, or
> it means something to me, but I'll leave it at that.

Sorry to press, but it would be useful information.
Here are some of my concerns for truly systematic Glosa:

What are the equivalent Glosa idioms for the Ido suffixes?
Same for the top 100 words by frequency in English?
What are the semantic fields, corresponding with Roget's 1000?
Are there adequate meronyms for each category?
How is the genitive expressed, in all situations?
How are participles, if any, to be used?
How many and which verbs can act as auxiliaries?
How are determiners/quantifiers to be used?

Now I feel like venting some complaints, which is always fun:

Why are there so $#@! many short words that look
like particles but aren't, and long words that are?

Why is the principle of grammatical isolation abandoned
in a few cases? Inconsistency is inexcusable in an IAL.

Why isn't pure compounding used in the clumsy
anti-mnemonic "-BO -PE" series?

Why not jettison the ugly Greek roots altogether,
keeping only roots that appear in both English and Latin?

Should there be any participles or not?
Just one is plainly wrong, should be 0 or 2.

From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 5 Aug 2000 15:44:08 -0400 (EDT)

On Sat, 5 Aug 2000, Robin Gaskell wrote (excerpted, in reply to me):

> >> 'speak-as-you-look-it-up-in-the-dictionary' language,
> >
> >    Which I think is unrealistic, simply because people with different
> >native habits are going to look things up in different orders. [...]

> *   I refer to the 'phrase-book mentality' where people fill in the words
> they are not sure of, through reference to the dictionary.

    Thanks for the clarification.  This I can go with.

> >    In this I think he was only partially right.  While it is true that
> >children do not ordinarily learn from a formal grammar text, neither do
> >they learn free from context, including nonverbal cues.  The
> >paradigmatic sentence approach is all well and good as far as it goes,
> >but the problem is that it does not go far enough.  The context is
> >missing.  [...]
> >
> *   I feel that you are advocating more listening and short story reading
> in Glosa, rather than grammatical study.

    If one is going to learn Glosa (or any other language, for that
matter) by paradigmatic examples rather than by formal exposition,
then, yes, I think there needs to be more connected text.  As for the
listening, for many of us, at the beginning, at least, most use of an
IAL will be through writing and reading.  I think there should be both
approaches, paradigmatic material and formal exposition.  Without at
least some of the latter, a lot of people are not going to take Glosa
seriously.

    There is the matter, which has been brought up before, of teaching
materials.  As I have claimed earlier, I think that it *is* possible to
make a good start on a language like Glosa with a booklet containing
examples and wordlists, just a little more ample than the existing
G.E.O. materials.

>     And on the question of standardisation, I would use a different
> analogy.  During my Secondary Schooling, the MKS system of Physical Units
> was adopted: life was much more straightforward after this, by contrast
> with the mixed systems of units that existed before-hand.

    (Although this is a side issue, I would actually agree.  I have
long been in favor of the Metric System, as we call MKS here in
Yankee-dom.  In fact, it has been *legal* to use it here in the US 
for over a century -- it is just not required.  Winess the nonsense
that if I buy a liter of imported olive oil, it is marked both as a
liter and as 33.8 US fluid ounces, which is stupid, considering that 
it would be fully legal to mark it as one liter and leave it at that.)

    *** Incidentally, I thought it was very worthwhile to reproduce
Wendy Ashby's letter the other day.  It was a good example of what the
language is capable of.  Unfortunately, I pruned my mailboxes too
vigorously and did away with it.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 5 Aug 2000 15:51:28 -0400 (EDT)

On Fri, 4 Aug 2000, Charles wrote (excerpt):

> An isolating language *can* be as systematic as desired.
> I think Glosa can be improved "merely" by reforming its
> vocabulary,

    I concur.  I think that the structure of the language is
satisfactory, "good enough" in the phrase associated with me, as it is. 
All that it really needs is more judicious use of vocabulary material
WHICH ALREADY EXISTS.  As those who follow the AUXLANG list will know,
I think that the quest for greater "perfection" in a constructed IAL is
in vain and may even be counterproductive.  One person's necessary
feature is another person's fundamental flaw.  A feature that is easy
to one person is difficult to another, whereas a different feature is
easy to the second and difficult to the first.  And so on and on and
on.  I doubt that it is practically feasible to devise some language
worth using which in all particulars will be equally "easy" to
everybody.  As far as I am concerned, the way Glosa is built is
adequate.  All that it really needs is some sprucing up in vocabulary
selection.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Consistency in a Constructed IAL
From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 5 Aug 2000 16:27:30 -0400 (EDT)

    I think that I pressed the wrong key and wiped out a message I
intended to send to the list.  If in fact it shows up, please pardon
the repetition.

    I was responding to Charles, who said that inconsistency is
inexcusable in an IAL.  I do not entirely agree.  Auxiliary language
design involves a lot of trade-offs due to competing values.  It may
not be completely possible to satisfy all desiderata simultaneously. 
Something may have to give way at times, and in the process what seems
to be an "inconsistency" may be allowable in order to accomplish
something else.  I do not have a specific example in mind, only that 
do not completely agree with Charles on this point.

    There is also an esthetic element, although this aspect is
notoriously slippery.  I myself am not especially enamored of
Esperanto, but some people have called it a work of art.  J.R.R.
Tolkien referred to this, and also referred to some language he
identified only as "N**" (probably Novial) as "factory product" 
which, despite its alleged superiority, was less desirable (in his
opinion) than E-o.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Consistency in a Constructed IAL
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 05 Aug 2000 13:57:55 -0700

"Paul O. Bartlett" wrote:

>     I was responding to Charles, who said that inconsistency is
> inexcusable in an IAL.  I do not entirely agree.  Auxiliary language
> design involves a lot of trade-offs due to competing values.  It may
> not be completely possible to satisfy all desiderata simultaneously.
> Something may have to give way at times, and in the process what seems
> to be an "inconsistency" may be allowable in order to accomplish
> something else.  I do not have a specific example in mind, only that
> do not completely agree with Charles on this point.

Conscious trade-offs imply a kind of consistency to me,
so our disagreement is perhaps even less than first appears.
Perfection isn't possible or necessary. But here are a few
specific, curable Glosa inconsistencies that bother me:

The GE- past/passive participle ... Why not have an
active-present participle (AP)? (Actually some Glosa words
*do* have an AP embedded in the "root", which is to me
a sort of "inexcusable" "cheating".)

Why say "Glosa has no morphology" when it does have some?
It indicates foggy thought processes. (If any band of
guerrila language reformers wishes to purge *all*
morphology from Glosa, I might join.)

Similarly, the -BO set of unneeded affixes.

The genitives/possessives are sometimes treated like
descriptive adjectives, sometimes like determiners,
other times like prepositional phrases. Too fuzzy,
not "systematic" enough.

In the latest note from Wendy, it appears the awful
semicolon of Hogben has been dropped, hooray!
But it still looks like relexed Brit-English to me.
I guess that's a consistency, but the wrong type ...

The less grammar there is in the language, the more
important it is. Glosa might do well to clean it up a bit.
Alternatively, hope that users avoid mis-features and
converge, as if by the economist's "unseen hand". Or both.
