To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>
Date: Mon, 7 Aug 2000 22:24:53 +0200

Karo ami, 

(Well, yes, I know, it is silly, if I - with my low English skill - 
would work on an English dictionary.  But because I criticized 
around a few days ago, I should not only talk.  And so I start. 
...)

A new Glosa dictionary is available. Please visit: 

 - Glosa-English: www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gglen.htm 
               or www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gglen.txt.gz 
 - English-Glosa: www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gengl.htm 
               or www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gengl.txt.gz 

What is it? 

* Source was Bill Patterson's internet dictionary "Literary" (or 
    "Glosa All"). 
* Now it is formatted as an HTML-file with click-click-index 
    (gglen.htm and gengl.htm) and a plain ASCII-file (gglen.txt.gz 
    and gengl.txt.gz) for your personal formatting games.  Some 
    internet browsers decode the GNU-zip contraction automatically. 
* All three levels are in one file: 
    "Literary" is the source and it is completely contained. 
    Words from "Base"-dictionary (= all words without synonyms) are 
      marked with "+" (or "++"). 
    Words from "Core"-dictionary (= only most useful "Base"-words) are 
      marked with "++". 
  So a good overview of word preferences is given (although the 
  prefernces should change in advantage to Glosa 1000 in future). 
* New feature: 
    Glosa 1000 words are marked with "1". 
* To add the "1"-marks, I used the list "Glosa 1000 - English" from the 
    book "18 Steps to Fluency".  It has 1182 items.  (I know, there are 
    different lists of "Glosa 1000".  Even "English - Glosa 1000" in 
    the same book is not the same vocabulary.  I think, it does not 
    make much use to argue, wich list would be best for vocabulary 
    research.) 
* Looking up the "18 Steps"-list, 96 Glosa words have been found which 
    have not been in "Literary" before (mainly ending vowel 
    differences).  They are now added.  The words are listed in the 
    file www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/ggehist.txt.gz.  
* Looking up the "18 Steps"-list, about 170 English meanings to Glosa 
    words have been found which have not been in "Literary" before.  
    They are now added.  They are listed in .../ggehist.txt.gz, too. 
* As Wendy wanted, it is free available in WWW, but the source is 
    mentioned.  So, no password-locking is a hindrance to learn 
    Glosa. 
* Only the Glosa-English file is worked on.  The English-Glosa is a 
    simple computer reverse.  That could have caused errors. 
* Because I am stupid, new mistakes certainly were added.  Please find 
    them. 


What now? 

  The new file gives the possibility to access all Glosa words AND 
  to prefer Glosa 1000.  
  So, a text written with the internet dictionary can be understood by 
  a learner of printed vocabulary lists from Richmond.  Unfortunately 
  that was not so easy before. 
  
  Next step should be to supplement the list "English 5000 to 
  Glosa 1000" from the book "Central Glosa" to cover a bigger 
  meaning's field with the heart vocabulary. 

  Perhaps you have some suggestions how to do it.  As I wrote, my 
  scanning and OCR-text recognition failed (I tryed the program "Text 
  Bridge Pro 9.0").  Does anybody else want to have a try?  If it must 
  be typed in manually, does anybody want to help?  The "E5000G1000" 
  has 51 pages.  Thanks in advance. 

Saluta - Marcel <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] vocabulary, word-building
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 07 Aug 2000 15:27:55 -0700


Glosa was designed as an analytic/isolating language.
Instead of the grammaticalized suffixes of Esperanto,
which are difficult or impossible for the average human
to master, plain word-compounding (hyphenation)
is sufficient to communicate any idea.

That intention was not always maintained in Glosa.
Many words carry frozen suffixes clumsily in the root:
FUNCTIO, FUSIO, GENERALI, GLOBULA, etc among the
"basic" vocabulary, and much worse in the 6000-set:
FUNE/FUNEBRI, GAMBA/GAMBADO, GELATO/GELATINO.
Much better are the words like GLUKO, GRAFI, GREGA.

As I understand it, compounding is the only valid
word-building mechanism in a pure analytic/isolating
typology; probably no natural language has ever been
100% A/I, but that's how I want Glosa to be, so one
could truthfully say that "Glosa has no affixes".
This makes it maximally easy for beginners, who will
constitute over 90% of the "Glosa-pe" for a long time.
(Of course, the syntax must be kept simple as well.)

To make spontaneous word-building possible for the
beginner (at least), there must be some system to it.
Certain kinds of compounds are so frequent ("productive")
that they can be listed, and mastered easily. Glosa's
so-called generics are a very poor set in my opinion,
both because they are too short (-BO -PE etc) and
there aren't nearly enough of them. Also, they're ugly!

http://www.hum.uva.nl/~ewn/corebcs/BaseTypes.htm

This would be a better bunch of generics, in "standard"
CVCV form, IMO. There is even a lot more material to be
mined there; the EuroWordNet project is now completed.
They have identified the top concepts across several
eurolangs (8) and it is modern scientific work, not
the sort that Dr. Gode faked in the 1940's at IALA.

To the extent that Glosa has suitable words for these
60 or so items, some of them coinciding with the generics,
there would be no change or additions needed, just a
presentation of them as useful compounding elements.

Then, a fuller examination of the 1000 or so "common
base concepts" would fill in the holes of Glosa's vocab.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] vocabulary, word-building
From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 7 Aug 2000 20:47:32 -0400 (EDT)

On Mon, 7 Aug 2000, Charles wrote:

> Glosa was designed as an analytic/isolating language.
> Instead of the grammaticalized suffixes of Esperanto,
> which are difficult or impossible for the average human
> to master, plain word-compounding (hyphenation)
> is sufficient to communicate any idea.
> 
> That intention was not always maintained in Glosa.
> [remainder cut for space]

    I myself am not so concerned about purity in conformity with an
ideal as with what works in practice.  Indeed, has that not been said
about Occidental?  As for whether an intention as an analytic/isolating
language has been maintained in Glosa, I think it it has approached
close enough as to be acceptable.  The prefixes and suffixes of which 
I take it that Charles disapproves are really, in my estimation, just
shortened forms of compounding elements.  Are the "-bo" series of
formants ugly?  Well, ugly is in the eyes of the beholder.  I don't
think they are ugly.

    One think we must beware of for constructed auxiliary languages 
is the quest for perfection.  It is a vain delusion and a snare.  One
person's necessary feature is another person's fatal flaw.  We will
never satisfy everybody.  Period.  If Esperanto were to become widely
used as an international auxiliary language, I would support it.  It 
is certainly a far sight easier than English, which is the most widely
used international lauxiliary language in history.  Of course, I think
we can do better than Esperanto, but in the end it comes down to
drawing lines, and people in the large will never draw them in the
same places.  I agree with Mario Pei that the quest for perfection can
actually be the enemy of the auxiliary language movement.  Good enough
is good enough, and except for vocabulary selection, I think Glosa is
good enough structurally as it stands.

    Learning vocabulary has always been my bugaboo.  I recently
finished a three-term course in Latin.  I had no difficulties with the
grammar (including, for the most part, the morphology).  Retaining
vocabulary was the hard part for me.  Indeed, if I did not have such 
a difficult time with vocabulary, and more leisure time to prepare
(something now in short supp;ly for me), I would try to write more in
Glosa.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] vocabulary, word-building
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 07 Aug 2000 18:40:09 -0700

"Paul O. Bartlett" wrote:

>     I myself am not so concerned about purity in conformity with an
> ideal as with what works in practice.  Indeed, has that not been said
> about Occidental?

In this case (Glosa) I think greater purity leads to greater ease
in practice. But in the case of Occidental, which seeks to leverage
the (European) learner's native language instincts, a quest for
purity is even sillier than usual when speaking of language.

I still want more truth-in-advertising: Glosa currently *does*
have a certain amount or morphology/inflection, but claims that
it doesn't. This, like the statement "Glosa has no grammar",
is at least a tactical error, reducing its credibility.

If it is possible to use Glosa-sin-morphology, I think it
works better ... and is more linguistically "interesting".
I accept the point about perfection being unattainable,
and even divisive in the unhappy history of auxlangs.

Subject: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: William W Patterson <ailanto{at}neosplice{dot}com>
Date: Tue, 08 Aug 2000 23:03:47 -0400

Je lundo, 7 augusto 2000 22:24:53 +0200, vi skribis:

> A new Glosa dictionary is available. Please visit: 
> 
>  - Glosa-English: www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gglen.htm 
>                or www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gglen.txt.gz 
>  - English-Glosa: www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gengl.htm 
>                or www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gengl.txt.gz 
 

One item, probably left over from a scanning error a long time ago...

   "adelo +   uncIear" in gglen.htm

   "uncIear   adelo +" in gengl.htm

Should be "unclear" {not evident; not obvious}.

(That's all you need to read of this post. The rest is just a whine.)

(Well, wait, read this bit too...)

Marcel, is the G1600 going to be posted separately, and/or merged into
your gglen and gengl? I'd like to see both, with G1600 folded into gglen
and gengl, thus continuing the evolution of the NetGlosa we've pondered
before on this list. But that's probably going to result in some more
irregularities. Glosalistas may want to take a look at my old criteria
page...

   http://www.neosplice.com/~ailanto/criteria.htm

where I outlined some of my ideas in re Glosa word selection.

And watch out for those final vowels! (What *should* we do with those
things? As somebody once pointed out (Paul?), they're almost like a
gender because you have to learn what vowel terminates each word. Except
that its a useless bunch of genders because they have no effect on
anything else in the sentence. We can't just drop them because that
would be harder to pronounce *and* there are still some words that
differ only in the final vowel. Probably best not to worry about them
too much, but also to not take them too seriously!)

(Okay, now the whine begins...)

Which also brings to our attention another of those multilingual and
prefix-that-isn't-a-prefix problems of the Glosa vocabulary... "a(d)"
in the sense of "at, to, towards" (explicit) versus "a" in the sense
of "not" (implicit). Eek! In Glosa, "delo" is "clear" and "adelo" is
"unclear"... but "a-" is *not* "un-". Things like this make it *very*
difficult to grasp Glosa's vocabulary.

One could argue that even though the "a" in "adelo" *looks* like a
prefix to somebody with prefixes in mind, it isn't. Can't be. Glosa
doesn't prefix that way. If it were a prefix, the word would be
"a-delo", which is obviously something other than "adelo". Still...
what to make of these words?...

re[+]    again; return to beginning
pro[+]   forwards
recede   go back
procede  go forward
cede     cede; yield
ki       go; move
ex-ki    leave

The "ki"/"ex-ki" pair looks okay. But it seems that "recede" and
"procede" should be spelled with hyphens. Except that "cede" is not "go"
(though it does imply movement of some sort). Eek again! Maybe we should
discard "recede" and "procede" and add "re-ki" and "pro-ki". Or simply
discard "recede" and "procede". Or something.

Maybe we should discard "adelo". We should use "ne delo" or "ne-delo"
or "no-delo" instead. Ah! That would be one way of simplifying and
regularising the vocabulary: eliminate all unique opposite and negative
words. In the basic dictionary, anyway.

I still think the vocabulary would have been better if they had chosen
Latin *or* Greek rather than Latin *and* Greek. That duality is the
source of many problems.

Glosa's grammar is its strong point; its vocabulary needs some work.

What is English "pity" in Glosa? "miseri". What is English "pitiless"
inÂ Glosa? "neleo"! For a literary dictionary I suppose that's okay, but
for basic use it's overly irregular. One might almost think of Glosa as
having five ineffective genders and thousands of inflections.

("miseri manko" would probably be my preference for "pitiless")

And what is Glosa "leo" in English? "lion"! I know, no hyphen in
"neleo", so I guess we're safe there. Inspiration for some wordplay!
>From "miseri" to "neleo" to "ne-leo" to "leo".

- Salata! - Bill

William W. Patterson                         http://www.neosplice.com/~ailanto
mailto:ailanto{at}neosplice{dot}com                 http://members.xoom.com/ailanto

If you've got a heart, then Gumby's a part of you!

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ge-
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Thu, 10 Aug 2000 22:03:01 +1000

At 23:05 31/07/2000 +0930, Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:
>Robin Gaskell wrote:
>
>>     While Ron Clark had a preference for the Greek roots, I try to remain
>
>That's probably not fair (or too fair) to Ron Clark.  Wasn't the
>inclusion of Greek roots a key part of the vision of Bodmer/
>Hogben?
>
*   I know Hogben wished to see suitable roots from both Greek and Latin,
but what I said about Ron Clark is true.  He may have started out with
Glosa unbiassed, but as time passed, he did favour Greek roots over Latin
ones, claiming that the Greek roots were muuch better for derivation of
terms and for the making of compounds, because of the superior
"agglutinative" qualities of the Greek roots.
eg  He favoured ~syn~ over ~ko~ = with
           and ~hidro~ over ~aqa~ = water.

>One of their arguments, I think, was that it would assist the
>inclusion of scientific words in the future language.
>
*   And this is where I came in.  When I met Ron, in 1977, I was told that
Glosa was an 'International Language for Science.'

>Some other IALs have chosen roots from the languages most largely
>spoken in Europe this (20th) century.  What is most common among
>them often turns out to have come from Latin (because of the
>combined weight of English and the romance languages), so they get
>about the same outcome as the Interlingua policy of taking direct
>from Latin.
>
*   The directions are from opposite ends: the majority of euro-auxlangs
start from an acceptance of the status quo in modern Euro-languages, and
selects vocabulary from this source; Ron sought the most common present-day
words, and then projected backwards from here to the roots of these common
words, and built the glosa lexicon from there.
>But also, since science coinings are generally international, and
>often Greek-based, that approach of taking from modern languages
>seems to achieve the objective that was hoped to come from
>directly Greek-based roots.
*   This seem to have been something that Ron did discover from his
researches, also.
>
>> neutral in this; however, I do definitely enjoy the availability, in
>> the broader application of the language, of a prime word from one 
>> Classical source, and a synonym from the other.
>
*   For me this is still true: I recognise the need for solidity in the
Learner's Vocabulary, with only a word a concept; but, for satisfying
communication, I have felt the need, personally, for the enhanced lexicon
giving the flexibility and avoidance of repetiition that can only come with
the presence of adequate synonyms.

Saluta,
  Robin

>>One piece of curiosity: when you write in English and in Glosa, do you
>>think in the language you are using, or do you think in Russian, and
>>then do a mental translation into the target language?
>>
>When I write  in English, I sometimes think in English and sometimes in
>Russian (For example this sentenece I thought In English, Russian and one
>word "For example" in Esperanto...)
>And when I write in Glosa I think only in Russian or in English, because
>my Glosa is very bad.
>
*   Please, Alexander, avoid such negative mental conditioning; your mind
is not yet fluent in thinking in Glosa.
    As far as I am concerned, the real secret about Glosa is learning to
thinkin a fairly objective (non-idiomatic) way about what you intend to
say.  After this, run snippets of phrases through your mind to see how they
"feel".  The next bit is to form complete phrases, so that when you say
them to yourself they retain the original meaning you intended .. and your
mind gets the "sensation" of the original intention, without thinking of
the actual language you are using.  After being happy with the phrase
stage, ^clicking^ these together to make full sentences in faily easy.
    Using clauses takes a little more work, because you are asking the
brain to hold, at one time, a number of more complex ideas, plus their
inter-connections.  Doing this all in Glosa requires a reasonable memory of
the necessary Glosa words, plus a fair understanding of the syntax
structures, and a facility with the phrasal structures associated with the
different words.
  EG     a fe domi       ex plu boteka      merca u sito

    fe pa ki a fe domi    veni ex plu boteka     seqe merca u sito

       Seqe merca u sito, e veni ex plu boteka, fe pa ki a fe domi. 

>   .....

>Now I am reading "18 steps", It is very Interest.
>
*   Glad you like it.  You might wish to put some of the questions, and
your answers to them, on Glosalist.
    This is a very basic level of instruction in Glosa.  Let me know when
you need further exercises, and I will attempt to supply them.

Saluta,
  Robin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] final vowels
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 11 Aug 2000 12:09:47 -0700


> U fini vouela es de uti, id face klare u litera avanti id.

Ja, akorda!

> U fini vouela pote sone homo ali hetera sed si id distingu plu verba, id sio
> ge tena. Exampla tena - tenu

Mali, kako! (I exaggerate for comic effect.)

A great advantage of analytic/isolating typology
is the ease of adding vocabulary from any source,
as long as the basic CVcv phonology (initial stress)
is not violated. I was just thinking of this:

SUtra ... a small strand/thread/line of thought

It doesn't matter whether such a term is pronounced
with a proper /a/ or not, unstressed vowels are
indistinct in speech and can't be heard amid noise.
However, Brits really must pronounce R somehow ...

To permit lexical differences marked only by one final
(unstressed) vowel is a flat-out loser of an idea.
English speakers cannot pronounce -e /e/ or -o /o/,
and will certainly (ab-)use schwa for -a /a/.
Lose the English speakers, and Glosa has no base.

The solution is to bow to linguistic reality and let
the finals "float", i.e. vary in spelling. They're
purely cosmetic, for ease and grace of pronunciation.
The few Glosa pairs like BALI/BALE should be fixed.

Occidental mainly abandoned use of final vowels for
syntactic use ala Esperanto/Ido. It was a good move;
De Wahl wrote about the phonological difficulties of
unstressed syllables such as -va/-ra/etc for tense,
which led him to use analytical auxiliaries ha/va
just as Glosa uses pa/fu. Convergent evolution ...

I went looking for Glosa words containing Y
and there sure aren't many ...

The 33 words in J look like this:

JA yes
JACE lie down {to}
JAKETA jacket
JAKULA throw
JAM already
JANUA door; entrance
JANUARI January
JARDINA garden {to}
JEJUNO fast {to}
JENTA breakfast {to}
JIGA jig
JOIEL jewel
JOKO joke; joke {play a}
JOKULA juggle
JOVIALI jovial
JU-AN boy
JU-FE girl
JUBA mane
JUBILA joy-shout
JUDIKA decide; judge
JUGA join; yoke
JULI July
JUNGLA jungle
JUNI June
JUNKTI joined
JUNKUS rush {plant}
JURA swear oath
JURGA quarrel
JURNALI newspaper
JURNALISTI journalist
JUSTI fair {equitable}; just {equitable}
JUVE youth; youthful
JUVENI young
JUXTA next to

Um, now guess how J is supposed to be pronounced?

Not many X's or even Z's or Q's.

Is W now allowed or not?

In an auxlang designed for world use,
where rapid acquisition is the #1 priority,
why not simplify a little where possible?
Some light-hearted proposals:

J ... to be pronounced as /dZ/ (English "J")
      *or* as /Z/ (French "J")
      *or* as /j/ (English "Y"), as at present

Y ... to be eliminated from Glosa

Z ... to be merged with S, Z itself eliminated
      (as in the Costa-Gavras movie)

C ... to be eliminated, replaced by K or S

Q ... replaced by KW xor KV (either but not both)

W ... maybe better just use V instead

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] final vowels
From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 11 Aug 2000 21:42:36 -0400 (EDT)

On Fri, 11 Aug 2000, Charles wrote (excerpted):

> It doesn't matter whether such a term is pronounced
> with a proper /a/ or not, unstressed vowels are
> indistinct in speech and can't be heard amid noise.

    I do not agree.  We English speakers -- Americans, at least; I will
not claim for others -- must realize that just because we have slovenly
speech habits, that is not an excuse for allowing or expecting
slovenliness in an auxiliary language.  Does all the rest of the world
have to conform to our sloppiness?  Might not others in the world
justifiably expect *us* to tighten up a little instead?  For that
matter, some languages speak syllables more or less with equal stress,
so there need not necessarily even *be* such a thing as an unstressed
syllable in an auxiliary language.  Let us not be seduced into taking
our native habits as always some sort of universals.

    English, despite its heavily latinate vocabulary, is structurally 
a germanic language in which, to some extent, consonants are more
important than vowels.  But in other languages, vowels have much more
importance than they do in English.  To speakers of such languages,
fine distinctions of vowels which to us are absurd are to them quite
normal.  Maybe everybody needs to be willing to give a little, and for
us that might mean more care with vowels than we are accustomed to.

> However, Brits really must pronounce R somehow ...

    However, English speakers really must pronounce vowels better
somehow.

> To permit lexical differences marked only by one final
> (unstressed) vowel is a flat-out loser of an idea.

    Perhaps only to speakers of languages in which vowel distinctions
are less important than consonant distinctions.  See above.  I simply
do not agree.  All I want is consistency across wordlists.

> English speakers cannot pronounce -e /e/ or -o /o/,
> and will certainly (ab-)use schwa for -a /a/.
> Lose the English speakers, and Glosa has no base.

    Then let's scrap Glosa (and every other constructed auxlang) 
and have everybody speak English.  After all, English is the most 
successful auxiliary language in history.  Why not stick with a 
winner?

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] final vowels
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 12 Aug 2000 12:46:14 +0930 (CST)

Paul O. Bartlett wrote:

> On Fri, 11 Aug 2000, Charles wrote (excerpted):
> 
> > It doesn't matter whether such a term is pronounced
> > with a proper /a/ or not, unstressed vowels are
> > indistinct in speech and can't be heard amid noise.
> 
>     I do not agree.  We English speakers -- Americans, at least; I will
> not claim for others -- must realize that just because we have slovenly
> speech habits, that is not an excuse for allowing or expecting
> slovenliness in an auxiliary language.  Does all the rest of the world
> have to conform to our sloppiness?  Might not others in the world
> justifiably expect *us* to tighten up a little instead?  For that
> matter, some languages speak syllables more or less with equal stress,
> so there need not necessarily even *be* such a thing as an unstressed
> syllable in an auxiliary language.  Let us not be seduced into taking
> our native habits as always some sort of universals.
> 
>     English, despite its heavily latinate vocabulary, is structurally 
> a germanic language in which, to some extent, consonants are more
> important than vowels.  But in other languages, vowels have much more

I don't think that's relevant.  Dutch is a germanic language.  
Compare the English (language) and Dutch pronunciations of
"Haarlem".  In both cases the stress is on the first syllable.  
In English, the vowel pronounced between the L and the M is the
one that is pronounced between the B and the L of "able".  In
Dutch, what is heard between the L and the M is the vowel (short)  
E pronounced with absolute clarity.

The issue with an IAL is that it has to be defined, and then there
has to be conformity with the definition, because that's all there
is, that's what the language is, that is the whole universe.  The
pronunciations of the vowels are specified.  You pronounce them
according to specification.  No ifs or buts or sometimes.  You
have to assume that the definition of the IAL is complete.  You
don't swallow vowels because no method of doing that, no protocol
as to when to do it, and no corresponding pronunciation, has been
specified.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] final vowels
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 11 Aug 2000 20:27:28 -0700

"Paul O. Bartlett" wrote:

>     I do not agree.  We English speakers -- Americans, at least; I will
> not claim for others -- must realize that just because we have slovenly
> speech habits, that is not an excuse for allowing or expecting
> slovenliness in an auxiliary language.

I wouldn't call it slovenly though; we simply stress our first
syllables strongly in good Germanic style. Glosa's phonology
depends upon it; so it is built-into both English and Glosa.
The particles seem to get a secondary stress. This is why
you *can* hear those hyphens in compounds. Without the strong
stress pattern, it would all just turn to mush.

> some languages speak syllables more or less with equal stress,

Yes, but Glosa has stress-timing rather than syllable-timing.

>     English, despite its heavily latinate vocabulary, is structurally
> a germanic language in which, to some extent, consonants are more
> important than vowels.

Same for Italic languages, because Indo-European was even more so.
In the latinate vocabulary, consonants carry roughly 16 bits
per word, vowels 4 bits. But by not populating all the possible
combinations, it becomes easier to distinguish speech (redundancy).
Having chosen 5 vowels and 15-20 consonants, inevitably the C's
are more important than V's ... and then etymology of the roots
just reinforces that tendency. No way we could change the
first vowel in most roots; and no way to remember the second.
I'm only arguing for ease of learning and use -- it matters!
(Actually, the most vocalic language I've heard was Tahitian,
and even there the consonants dominate.)

> Maybe everybody needs to be willing to give a little, and for
> us that might mean more care with vowels than we are accustomed to.

Yes, and even more so for consonants; L/R is difficult for many.
Given the latinate bias, we are stuck with both F/V.
But there is little need or use for the S/Z distinction.

> All I want is consistency across wordlists.

That is certainly achievable. But I'll never be able to
memorize etymologically wrong final vowels, and nobody
who knows "silvi" will fail to understand "silva".

> > Lose the English speakers, and Glosa has no base.
> 
>     Then let's scrap Glosa (and every other constructed auxlang)
> and have everybody speak English.  After all, English is the most
> successful auxiliary language in history.  Why not stick with a
> winner?

One might want to co-opt the leader to win in the marketplace.
Nobody but a few Americans and British have heard of Glosa,
so they must be coddled a little. If it were difficult to do,
then it wouldn't be worth the effort, but this is so easy.

"In Glosa, final vowels don't matter, they just sound nice."

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] basic dictionary
From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 12 Aug 2000 21:39:00 -0400 (EDT)

On Sat, 12 Aug 2000, Charles wrote:

> Does anyone have any suggestions as to how to select
> the words which will go into the Glosa basic dictionary,
> or any criteria to apply during the process?

    There are several wordlists which could serve as at least
preliminary guides.  Rick Harrison, with assistance from others,
compiled what I think he may have called his Universal Language
Dictionary (ULD).  I don't have a current URL.  A couple of years ago,
I collated the wordlists of Basic English, Essential World English, and
the Longman Defining Vocabulary.  The collated list in ASCII text
format is at ftp://ftp.smart.net/pub/bartlett/base.vocabulary .

    I do not suggest that somehow there be some sort of slavish one 
for one word substitution between a basic Glosa dictionary and such
wordlists (especially the one I collated, because E.W.E. may rely on
some of the principle of Basic English, which I consider basically
fraudulent).  However, if a Glosa dictionary has sufficient vocabulary
to express what these sorts of lists express, then I would think well
of it.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] final vowels
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sun, 13 Aug 2000 21:00:08 +1000

At 20:37 11/08/2000 -0700, Charles wrote:
>Michael Talbot-Wilson wrote:
>
>> The issue with an IAL is that it has to be defined, and then there
>> has to be conformity with the definition,
>
>Yes, once Glosa becomes "frozen" in its design ...
>but as it is, Glosa looks quite unfinished to me.
>Are W and Y in the alphabet? Undefined.   .....
*    These are in my understanding transitional, residual forms:
The "Y" comes in a few words, mainly from Greek, and will ultimately be
replaced by an "I".
     "W" occurs in very few Classical roots, so will not be seen in most
Glosa texts.  I cannot see that there need be a special ruling on either of
these letters.
     Because the Glosa treatment of proper nouns has them rendered as they
are in the original language, then these 'strange' letters will still be
used for Place names and "foreign" personal names.  EG  William Healy pa
habe meso-vora ko an amika, Yonny Zerow, ex Woyabi intra Zambia.

     I think these letters can be 'tolerated.'

Saluta,
  Robin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] alphabet
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sun, 13 Aug 2000 22:44:32 +1000

At 12:41 11/08/2000 -0700, Charles wrote:
>
>I went looking for Glosa words containing Y
>and there sure aren't many ...
>
>The 33 words in J look like this:
>
>JA yes
>   .....
>JUXTA next to
>
>Um, now guess how J is supposed to be pronounced?
>
*   Yes, this does seem a bit odd: is the kid next door Yonni Yones, or
Dzonni Dzones, or do we just allow him the idiosyncracity of keeping his
strange Anglo name, Jonny Jones, with its odd, Anglo, quirky "J"?

>In an auxlang designed for world use,
>where rapid acquisition is the #1 priority,
>why not simplify a little where possible?
>Some light-hearted proposals:
>
*   I believe that Sydney's Wooloomooloo could be rendered ~Vulumulu~ in
Glosa, but something of the local colour would be lost .. for the sake of 
'forced' standardisation.

>J ... to be pronounced as /dZ/ (English "J")
>      *or* as /Z/ (French "J")
>      *or* as /j/ (English "Y"), as at present
>
*   The last, but only in 'native' Glosa words, otherwise as it is
pronounced in the "ethnic" proper noun.

>Z ... to be merged with S, Z itself eliminated
>      (as in the Costa-Gavras movie)
>
*   Natives of Glosoidia would be able to tell the difference: others,
whose linguistic development precluded any difference, would just have to
fudge it; while those, able to tell the difference, on receiving the
garbled /sz/, would subconsciously ''hear'' the right letter being sounded.

>C ... to be eliminated, replaced by K or S
>
*   There is NO /sh/ in Glosa, so perhaps the ~c~ could be interpreted as
either the "SH" or "CH" of English, while, of course, purists from
Glosoidia would know it should be the soft /ch/.   If, however, ~c~ is
redefined to be either a /k/ or the soft /s/, then both /sh/ and soft /ch/
have been lost from Glosa.

>Q ... replaced by KW xor KV (either but not both)
>
*   A form of logic demands such a replacement.  But I, personally, do not
like such forced, un-historic logic.  But also, the mystical "Q" with its
mysterious tail, which may be a path leading into .. or is it out of .. the
circle, has that misty kwerulous kwality denoting the "unknown".
  EG  Qe, es, alo ne es?
      [question: be or not be]
      To be or not to be.

>W ... maybe better just use V instead
>
*   Yes, some would pronounce the Glosa native ~w~ as a /v/ anyway, but, so
far, I have not detected any such native Glosa ~w~s.  And, of course, the
same would apply to the pronunciation of non-native "W"s.  
   EG  ~Pa-di mi pa vide u Vim Venders film "Vings Over Berlin".~

Saluta,
  Robin

At 12:09 11/08/2000 -0700, Charles wrote:

>> U fini vouela es de uti, id face klare u litera avanti id.

Plus, u-ci difere habe lexiko signifi:

   an = he
   ana = up.

>> U fini vouela pote sone homo ali hetera sed si id distingu plu verba, >>
id sio ge tena. Exampla tena - tenu
>
>Mali, kako! (I exaggerate for comic effect.)
>
>   .....
>
>The solution is to bow to linguistic reality and let
>the finals "float", i.e. vary in spelling. They're
>purely cosmetic, for ease and grace of pronunciation.
>The few Glosa pairs like BALI/BALE should be fixed.
>
*   Veri, mi pa memo Ron Clark ki dia u homo line de puta.  Prima an 
pa dona libera ad u fini vokali-litera: seqe, an detekti ma verbi de an pra
dona provide; po-co, an pa decide ke an nece habe kontrola de plu-ci fini
vokali-litera.
    Koncerne un exempla; ge-dona ci, mi nota - intra _Glosa 6000_ -
tena [hold] e tenuo [thin,tenuous].  Posi, intra plu hetero lista id es
~tenu~.  Qe, u-ci dupli vokali kausa dificili?  Mi puta: ne.
    Koncerne plu bi versio de ~bal-~, mi habe akorda: il sio es solo mo
forma.  Na habe tro verbi ko simi semani, ex u ~ba-~ radika:
   Ko u baci, an pa bate u bola; qi pa gene ge-bali ad an. 
     N.B.  ~pra~ (Paleo Glosa, ex ~pre pa~) = "had" 

>Occidental mainly abandoned use of final vowels for
>syntactic use ala Esperanto/Ido. It was a good move;
>De Wahl wrote about the phonological difficulties of
>unstressed syllables such as -va/-ra/etc for tense,
>which led him to use analytical auxiliaries ha/va
>just as Glosa uses pa/fu. Convergent evolution ...
>
*   Veri so, konverge evolve de puta.

Saluta,
  Robin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] basic dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 13 Aug 2000 10:53:14 -0700


I took Paul's list of approx 2700 EWE-etc words,
mangled and merged it with Rick Harrisons VITAL list
of approx 2300, and made a single list of 3028
"vital and essential" English words and affixes.
A piece of it (pla- words) looks like this:

      3 place
      3 plain
      3 plan
      3 plane
      2 planet
      3 plant
      3 plastic
      3 plate
      1 platform
      3 play

1 = appears only in Paul's list (625 items)
2 = ditto Rick's (340 items)
3 = is in both (2113 items)

This was easy to do using standard Unix/Linux tools
"sort/uniq" etc. I did simplify complex entries a bit,
e.g. "a/an" was separated into "a" and "an". Only
individual words were kept, e.g. "in favour of" was
dropped, "favour" kept. Perfection is unattainable!
Are there too many? I don't think so, because many are
derivatives; I'm surprised the total is so low:

      3 indoor
      1 indoors

      1 industrial
      3 industry

      3 infect
      1 infection
      1 infectious

      1 infer
      1 inference

I think it's pretty good for its purpose.
Please check it out:

http://catty.com/~catty/lang/vitaless.txt

"index" es plu mi lexiko
GE1K(SPdate) e GE6K(SPdate)
Mi volu translati ge1k ad in plu hetero lingua e face g6k ge explika in g1k.
Na fu nece solo lexiko de g1k translati multi kron.
Qe plu grafo pe qi pa face u internet lexiko posi lase mi 
download u mu ergo a u mi puta-me?
Mi fu nece kopi plu English-lingua text file e face u puta-me programa de 
face u tedio ergo pro mi.     
Syd


kopi=copy=^face/imita/ 
lexiko=lexicon=^bibli/translati/verbi_a_verbi/lingua_a_lingua
tedio=tedium=^emotio/multi_no-interese_itera/triste
translati     translati=translate=^face/lingua/mo_a_hetero

ad=at   ad=to   de=about e=and ergo=work   es=is   
explika=explain   face=make fu=shall ge=got(passive)   
grafo=write   hetero=different   in=in kron=instant   
lase=let lingua=language   lingua=tongue   me=device   
me=machine   mi=I   mi=me   mu=them   mu=they   multi=many   
na=us   na=we   nece=must   pa=past   pe=person   plu=more_than_one   
plu=plural   posi=might(doubt)   
posi=perhaps   pro=forward   pro=in_favour_of   pro=towards   
programa=program   puta=think   
qe=question_begins_yes/no_question   qi=who   solo=alone       spdate 
tri=three   u=a/the volu=want

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] alphabet
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 14 Aug 2000 20:18:09 -0700


> > *   There is NO /sh/ in Glosa,
> 
>     "18 Steps to Fluency in Euro-Glosa," third edition, page 5, and
> other Glosa Education Organization publications: "SC always pronounced
> like Eng. SH.

Basic reference says:

        C always as CH in CHurCH;
        G always hard as in Go;
        J as the Y in Yes;
        Q as QU in QUick;
        R always sounded, trilled if possible;
        SC as the "sh" sound in neSCience.

There are few languages that distinguish CH SH K S and Z.
This is a needless difficulty in Glosa, which is otherwise
very easy to pronounce, more so than the other IALs.
There are not too many words using Z or SC that they
must be retained -- they even look archaic.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] thoughts about dictionaries
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 19 Aug 2000 10:19:28 -0700


I have collected several English word lists such as
Paul's and Rick Harrison's metalists, and several
well-known individual lists such as West's, Kilgariff's,
WordNet, etc. I sort and merge and edit them in various
ways to explore what use they might be in defining a core,
base, general, or full list of concepts needed for an IAL.
I've also tried this with collections of other IAL word
lists such as Eurolang, Ido, Lingua Franca, etc. What
it all amounts to is a huge table sort of like this:

LANG1    SOURCE1  WORD1      LANG2     SOURCE2  WORD2
English  WEST     talk       Vul.Lat.  Xxx      parle

This is symmetrical so it represents a pair of entries,
bidirectionally "parla <--> talk". The whole mess can then
be sorted in various ways, so for instance all the words
equivalent to "talk" appear together. Then if there is no
entry for Glosa it can be considered "missing"; or if only
Glosa has a word for it, then it might be considered
superfluous for the purpose of a core dictionary. (There
are some exceptions possible but I ignore them for now.)

Occidental is also in a state of not having a good online
dictionary. I strongly believe that there can and should be
some cooperation and cross-fertilization between IALs,
especially those derived from european languages. So this
may be pointing to a combined database of IAL word lists.
It would be too big for convenient online access, assuming
52k modems and ISP limits on space and CGI, but not for
a home PC with DSL, which is probably becoming the norm.

Further ideas related to Glosa? I don't intend to talk
without doing, or do without planning, so I'm asking ...

---------------------------------------------------------------------

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] thoughts about dictionaries
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick_hempshall{at}hotmail{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 20 Aug 2000 18:16:11 +0100

Charles grafo (mero)
>
> I have collected several English word lists such as
> Paul's and Rick Harrison's metalists, and several
> well-known individual lists such as West's, Kilgariff's,
> WordNet, etc. I sort and merge and edit them in various
> ways to explore what use they might be in defining a core,
> base, general, or full list of concepts needed for an IAL.

Sounds like a useful way to see if all the semantic field is properly
covered. If you do this you need to allow for the fact that there is not a
one to one correspondence between any two language (as you know), in fact
there is a many to many mapping involved here.

In particular, when looking at Glosa, by design, a Glosa word should map to
a number of English synonyms and near synonyms. (The Glosa authors claim up
to 100 English words might be covered by one Glosa word). And also that many
"concepts" represented in English by a single word are represented in Glosa
by a short "phrase" of two or more words.

Nick

To unsubscribe, e-mail: glosalist-unsubscribe{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
For additional commands, e-mail: glosalist-help{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] thoughts about dictionaries
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 20 Aug 2000 13:13:20 -0700


> you need to allow for the fact that there is not a
> one to one correspondence between any two language

This is very true, and unavoidable between any pair of languages.
One way to solve it is to use "synsets" to differentiate:

ENGLISH             GLOSA
spring, fountain    fonta
spring, elastic     elasti

> by design, a Glosa word should map to
> a number of English synonyms and near synonyms

That's the "word-building" or "derivational" facility.
I have many complaints about Glosa in this area, where
it is not faithful enough to its own principles:

ENGLISH         GLOSA
submarine       submarina
submerge        subomerge <--- subO
subscribe       subskribe <--- sub

Now, the "subo=under" and "merge/skribe" are nice,
so why not SUBOMERGE and SUBOSCRIBE? With disyllabic
roots, it is extremely easy to make such compounds,
and they are easy to recognize in actual speech,
whether or not hyphenated because of the stress.
I suppose SUBO (= INFRA) was just an error in the
materials, but it avoids consonant clusters.
Otherwise, ambiguously parsable words act like
frozen non-compoundable roots:

ENGLISH         GLOSA
sudden          subito
sublime         sublime
subject         subjekti

Glosa has quite a few roots suitable for compounding,
and of course those should go into basic vocab;
Greek used root+o+root extensively and productively,
and Greek is the basis of Glosa (for better and worse).

(Marcel, I lost your email address ... 3 more pages ready.)

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au

From: "Jay Bowks" <jjbowks{at}adam{dot}cheshire{dot}net>

No Catty. This is just not adding to Glosa's
use of internationally recognized words. To
Use something like SUBOSCRIBE is just plain
silly. Who in their right mind would come
up with such a term when the languages that
are at the base of glosa vocabulary omit the
O... why insist on putting things in to make
the language more artificial. Glosa is simple
and beautiful enough without all the faky made
up stuff. No I don't agree that there is any
need for a sounding vowel between the root.

Take the roots from Latin and Greek. Fonetize
them and make them work. It is simple and in
my eyes it is beautiful.

Since,
Jay B.

From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>

Marcel Springer wrote:
> 
> Place visita plu verba-lista

http://www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/

I took several (5) of the word lists, combined and sorted etc.
It looks like this (all the "cit-, civ-" items):

2       cita        quote, urge
1       citate      city
1       citi        swift
2       citri       lemon
1       citro       citrus, lemon
1       citronada   lemonade
1       citru       citrus
4       civi        citizen
4       civili      civil, courteous, courtesy, meek, mercy, polite
1       civili-an   gentleman
1       civili-fe   lady
3       civiliza    civilization, civilize
1       civismo     good citizenship
4       civita      city

The first column is the number of citations,
which helps point out typographical errors, and
which are the "core" words too, to some extent.
There are 8567 items in total, 5638 appear only once,
1401 appear twice, 1528 more than twice.

I am putting the file temporarily on my web area:
http://catty.com/~catty/lang/glosatest.dat

Columns are tab-separated, parenthetical info deleted.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 25 Aug 2000 01:23:50 -0700


> Next task should be the change of word preferences

I wonder how we should proceed, after getting familiar
with the material? For example, the FAV- words need
to be cleaned up ...

1       favi      honeycomb
2       favo      favor, favour, partial, privilege
2       favori    favorable, favour
2       favorita  favorite
1       favu      honey comb

... by selecting just 2 di-syllabic roots from proper Latin,
preferring the infinitive verb form wherever possible,
and converting -US/-UM to -O (and -IS to -I):

2  favus   FAVO   honeycomb
6  favere  FAVE   favor, favour

An alternative strategy might be to examine several modern
euro-languages instead of Latin. Then it would be "favo"
for both of the above, which is awkward, or just pick
FAVO/FAVI arbitrarily.

Then derivatives can be formed in straightforward manner.
I don't dislike final vowels when they are etymologically
sensible and phonologically easy to distinguish.

Of the 2 roots, only one is likely to be useful in
forming compounds, the other is a low-frequency item.
This could be indicated by bold-type or stars or something.
It would be nice to list the source word too.

This mail tells you A) how the Glosa words are built and B) how to 
learn them easily. 


A) Glosa words are built from (scientific) English. 
   e.g.: 
    English "graph-I-cs" = drawing  
    --->  Glosa word for "to draw" = "grafi".
    English "graph-O-logy" = science of psychological analysis of 
                               hand-WRITing 
    --->  Glosa word for "to write" = "grafo". 

B) And now how to learn Glosa words: 
   - Learn Latin                     !! 
       It is the source language. 
   - Learn ancient Greek             !! !! 
       It is a source language, too. 
   - Learn English                   !! !! !! 
       Necessary for the ending vowels. 
   - Hope                            !! !! !! !! 
       Hope, you find the mnemotechnic trick!


Please let us   S T O (P)   this situation!  

(Next mail with less cynicism.  I promise it.)

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Re: final vowels 
From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>
Date: Sat, 26 Aug 2000 15:58:23 +0200

This mail tries to tell A) how the Glosa words are built [this time 
less cynical and I really do not know B) how to learn them easily]. 


- Some words are derived regularly from the classical words, like 
  Peano suggested. 
  Indefinite words remain as they are (ad, de, si, Greek: kata). 
  -a, -ae get (from ablativus) -a (silva), 
  -us, -us get -u (manu), 
  -is, -is get -i (turi), 
  adjectives -us/-a/-um get -o (karo), 
  verbs with -ere get -e (face), with -are: -a (lauda), -ire: -i 
  (veni). 

- Same with greek.  Greek has no ablativus, so take dativus: 
  -os, -u gets -o (fobo, orto). 

- Greek verbs get -o (1st person singular) (skizo) 

- (I suppose: A regular system will cause too many -o-endings? 
  Perhaps the language would sound too masculine?  From Ninja-films 
  I know, Japanese has it the other way round: -a masculine and -o 
  feminine.  However:) 
  Latin words with -us, -i or -um, -i do NOT get -o, but ???. 

- Latin -io, -ionis do not get ablativus-ending -ione but keep the 
  nominativs -io (natio, okasio, petitio, religio, tensio). 
  [My personal opinion: -ione is too long, okay, but even -io is a 
  part-of-speech-marker.  Is it one syllable (-yo) or two (-ee-ow)? 
  No, there are two.  But then it is too long, I think.  A lot of 
  these words are derived from verbs.  "pete" and "tende"?] 

- (I suppose: Some Latin words are in international use mainly with 
  their perfect-tense-root.  So they took this root.  That could be
  reasonable.) 
  Words built from the perfect-tense-root get -i 
  (I suppose because of 1st person singular ending.) 
  (cepti, fluxi, komposi) 

- (I suppose for the same reason, international use:) Sometimes the 
  Greek aorist-root is taken instead of present-tense-root (gene). 

- Species names keep nominativus (equs, ursus). 

- See last mail: grafi from graphics and grafo from graphology.  This 
  was certainly a way to build some words.   Very interesting for this 
  is the book "Central Glosa".  E.g. (citation page 25) 
   "ami-able    ami    friend, -ly, kind 
    amo-ur      amo    love, like"
  (That is why the book is named "Central Glosa", because Glosa words 
  are in the centre.) 

- There is an article in "Plu Glosa Nota", no. 81 about deriving 
  Glosa words from Italian.  Unfortunately I have not got it.  Perhaps 
  it is interesting here.  Does anybody have it? 
  On page 2 of PGN 82 (this issue I have) there is written: 

    " Glosa E Italiano

      In PGN 81 na pa dice komo Glosa pote uti moderno Italiano de 
      gene id Mega lexiko. ...
      Posi Glosa pote prende plu participi pasato, - ..ATO, -ITO, -ETO 
      e -ABILI; kausa plu-ci parola-fini es proxi universali in plu 
      Euro-lingua.  ...
      Holo infinito ko -ARE, ERE, IRE diveni -A, -E, -I." 

- Perhaps you have more ideas about the secrets of Glosa words.  
  Please write. 
  (I suppose/fear, there is a secret about EVERY Glosa word, and no 
  real system for all.)

Ending vowels, word problems,- what to do? 

Perhaps we can start a new internet project "vocabulary research": 

  - We try to find a system for word building (like the list in one of 
    my previous mails, but better.) 
  - A maintainer puts a list of words to examine on a website. 
    This list could be "Glosa 1000", a new "Core"-dictionary or a 
    list of the ending-vowel-synonyms (silva/silvi, kara/karo ...) 
    or whatever anybody suggests. 
  - Everybody can help then.  Picking some words from the website, 
    looking up the Latin or Greek origin, looking up related words in 
    French, Spanish, Italian, ..., English, ..., Interlingua 
    dictionarys and collect them, making a suggestion of a better 
    Glosa word.  Then mailing it to the maintainer who collects all 
    entrys. 
  - Everybody can do as much as he wants.  If he has no Latin 
    dictionary, he may have an Italian.  Another has no Italian, but 
    a Greek.  Some wants to deal with old languages, some with 
    auxiliary languages and another wants only to make suggestions for 
    words.  The maintainer collects all and appends one comment to 
    a word to the previous comments to the same word and puts all 
    on the net. 
  - After a while we write all together and send it with a nice 
    letter to Wendy and hope she agrees or she writes other ideas. 
  - We put the new words into the Internet Dictionary, perhaps mark 
    them with asterisks as not original but to prefer. 

(Sorry, I cannot maintain such a list, because I have no direct access 
to my homepage.)

(Perhaps it is best to start with the ending-vowel-synonyms, because 
there Wendy can easily see that there must be changed something. )

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Re: dictionary 
From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>
Date: Sat, 26 Aug 2000 16:00:38 +0200

Charles wrote: 
 > > Next task should be the change of word preferences
 >
 > I wonder how we should proceed, after getting familiar ... 
(see dictionarys www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gglen.txt.gz , 
../gengl.txt.gz, gglen.htm, gengl.htm) 

  I would like to put the "+"s and "++"s to the "1"s 
(That means: prefer Glosa 1000 for the smaller dictionarys 
"Core"=++ and "Base"=+/++.).  

  This sounds simple, but it is not: e.g. 
 - After changing a "+" to an "1"-word in the English-Glosa-list, you 
     have to look up the Glosa-English-list for other related English 
     words.  There you will get other Glosa words, and so on. 
 - English homonyms (ring {bell}, ring {finger, ear})! 
 - Does the Glosa word really work like the English meaning? 
     E.g. "ab" is normally a preposition.  Or an adverb: "ki ab".  
     Does it really work to take it as an adjective "absent" or only 
     in a definite context?  Sometimes there is for one English word 
     no Glosa WORD but a Glosa DESCRIPTION given.  Can such a phrase 
     be equivalent to a word?  How do they behave in a word 
     compounding? 

  Does anybody see other traps?  And how to avoid or handle them?  
Please take the lists (../gengl.txt.gz and ../gglen.txt.gz) and have a 
try on your own! 

  For this work we need a new list of prefered English words.  
Because the current "Core" has now, after supplement of the 
"English 5000 to Glosa 1000", 3491 lines.  O.K, there are 
"honey comb" and "honeycomb", but they are too many. 

  I think about a list with about 2000 English words for "Core".  
Cleverly chosen, 2000 words are enough for standard situations and 
cover 85 % of English texts.  They will have less Glosa meanings, 
maybe 1600.  This vocabulary could be quite similar to "Glosa 1000" 
then.  

  We also need a new definition for "Base".  Former was: all words 
without synonyms (criterium English meanings, problem: English 
synonyms!).  Now, after supplement of E5kG1k, the list has 6884 lines. 
That is too much.  It should be 4000, or what do you suggest?

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>

>   I would like to put the "+"s and "++"s to the "1"s
> (That means: prefer Glosa 1000 for the smaller dictionarys
> "Core"=++ and "Base"=+/++.).
> 
>   This sounds simple, but it is not: e.g.

Well, I added at least 2 of the "basic" dictionaries,
so all the basic words should be 2 or greater, and it seems
in all cases this does happen. Most of the 1 entries are
either Glosa-6000 or errors. So the simple count is a very
good heuristic trick.

>  - English homonyms (ring {bell}, ring {finger, ear})!

That is why the best way to sort Glosa words is *not* by
their English equivalents; and just 1000 G's become 5000 E's.
Then you get a lot of entries like this:

5       acide   event, happen, incident, occur

These (ACIDE etc) are the best and most important Glosa words.
It is clear from the multiple English equivalents what the intended
meaning of the G root is. Then we don't worry about E homonyms
or vague meanings like this:

1       adveni  happen

>  - Does the Glosa word really work like the English meaning?

I hope not. Natural languages are too messy and complex
for the IAL purpose: a second language for all adults.

>   For this work we need a new list of prefered English words.

I don't know if this is true ... I found and combined some good
English learner lists, but if Glosa is truly systematic then
we should focus on Glosa. Comparisons with the Interlingua IED,
Occidental Radicarium, any Romance language dictionary, etc,
are very useful too.

But I think we now have the 1500-3000 words we need for
the New Revised Glosa Internet Dictionary!! Actually I would
prefer to keep all the entries (approx 8567) minus errors.
We just BOLD TYPE the basic 2000 or so.

>   We also need a new definition for "Base".

The top 1500-3000. Maybe we need 3 levels:
essential 1500, basic 3000, full 8000.

Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: final vowels
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>

> Ending vowels, word problems,- what to do?
> 
> Perhaps we can start a new internet project "vocabulary research":
> 
>   - We try to find a system for word building (like the list in one of
>     my previous mails, but better.)

We can do that as a Re-Neo-Glosa experimental project.
All living languages evolve, and we can help that process.
Lojban implemented a plan for future growth and reform.
Occidental is inherently mutable to adjust to user needs.
To freeze any language is either impossible or detrimental.

>   - Everybody can help then.

Radicals and reactionaries and moderates can all help.
We should make one restriction though, once we get organized:
all discussion about change must be in standard Glosa.
The first step is to clean-up the standard dictionary,
without any reforms, just collection and corrections.
Then we can try using the language, with translation
in English or some other. That's my suggestion.

>   - Everybody can do as much as he wants.  If he has no Latin
>     dictionary, he may have an Italian.  Another has no Italian, but
>     a Greek.  Some wants to deal with old languages, some with
>     auxiliary languages and another wants only to make suggestions for
>     words.  The maintainer collects all and appends one comment to
>     a word to the previous comments to the same word and puts all
>     on the net.

We can alternate the identity of the maintainer.
You organized the G1E5 project and it was successful.
I think my collected list is good, and if people here
accept it, then that task is finished. Somebody else
may lead the next step, whatever it may be.

>   - After a while we write all together and send it with a nice
>     letter to Wendy and hope she agrees or she writes other ideas.

Yes, an important step.

>   - We put the new words into the Internet Dictionary, perhaps mark
>     them with asterisks as not original but to prefer.

About adding new vocab ... I think we should explore the system
of derivation, so that users can make all the terms they need.
The G1E5 list shows the methods preferred by the authors.
Essentially it is phrasal-compounding in the pidgin style;
the arbitrary words like these are not good:

2  rentiera      renter, shareholder <-- use "-pe", not disguised "-ero"
1  reticentia    reticence <----- we have "tace" to make derivatives
1  retrospekti   look back <----- should be "retro-specta"

> (Perhaps it is best to start with the ending-vowel-synonyms, because
> there Wendy can easily see that there must be changed something. )

Good idea. Do the safe and easy things first.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: final vowels
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 26 Aug 2000 11:58:43 -0700


>  > 1       favi      honeycomb
>  > 2       favo      favor, favour, partial, privilege
>  > 2       favori    favorable, favour
>  > 2       favorita  favorite
>  > 1       favu      honey comb
> 
>   This list is a shame - full stop.

Easy to fix, though. After thinking more, it seems (to me) best
to do the least: keep just FAVO and FAVI, and change nothing.
We can do this for all 8500+ Glosa words and get a good baseline.

> Latin is good for systematicality, but without an eye on modern
> languages Glosa would be a step back in language history.
> Is not there a revival of Occidental (modern Romanic words) for the
> same reason, after some years the scientificality of Interlingua
> (systematical derived vocabulary from Latin) was said to be perfect.

Both Occidental/Interlingue (IE) and Gode's Interlingua (IA)
combined and regularized vocabulary from several European languages.
IE focused on vocab and never had time or money for a completely
finished grammar, OCC focused on grammar and never finished vocab.

Perhaps the ideal is to combine OCC/IA in the obvious way.
Then users could evolve the "new" language by simplification --
there are too many grammatical features and words for world use.

Glosa can, instead, begin with simple grammar and vocab,
learning from OCC, IA, Esperanto, Ido, and other IALs
Mainly what needs improvement is the derivational system.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: final vowels
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 26 Aug 2000 13:06:51 -0700


> - (I suppose: A regular system will cause too many -o-endings?

Too many collisions between common Latin words, e.g.:

mano/manare  ... flow, drip (eMANate)
maneo/manere ... remain, stay
mane (mana)  ... morning
manus        ... hand

No good way to resolve this traffic jam systematically.
There are more besides ... Glosa has lots of MAN- words:

4       mana      morning
1       manea     handle, manage
2       mania     frenzy, madness
1       manika    sleeve
1       manipula  manipulate
1       mano      rarefied
1       manovra   manoeuvre
5       manu      hand, hand over, pass {hand over}, smack
2       manubri   handle

Occidental has these MAN- roots:
maner = to remain, mani = many, manie = mania,
manier = manner, mann = man, manu = hand;
also: manna, manifest, manipular, manivelle, manovre.

>   Perhaps the language would sound too masculine?

No ... final vowels are always unstressed,
and rounded lips look cute on chicks. But Euros
don't like -u much, except maybe in Rumania.

> - Species names keep nominativus (equs, ursus).

Those -s words ... they bother me! Scientific words
(nomenclature) should be capitalized to show "foreignness".
Science is irrelevant to IAL use among common people,
and the scientists always make their own jargon.
Merely for propaganda, IALs invoke the word "science".
The correct terms are binomial, not just "Ursa".

> This was certainly a way to build some words.

>    "ami-able    ami    friend, -ly, kind

The method is good, but the components must be good too.
This is dangerously close to affixation, but it works ...
But tri-syllabic roots are not such good compounders.

ami-abili ... ability or worthiness to be loved
ami-agenti ... obvious, no?

> in "Plu Glosa Nota", no. 81 about deriving Glosa words from Italian

>   (I suppose/fear, there is a secret about EVERY Glosa word, and no
>   real system for all.)

All languages are unsystematic compromises, even Lojban, even
predicate calculus, even Java. But some are simpler and better
than others. I hear the linguistic police now at my door ...

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: final vowels
From: Michael Talbot-Wilson <mtw{at}birdseye{dot}view{dot}net{dot}au>

At 2000-08-26 11:06 -0700, Charles:

> 
> > Ending vowels, word problems,- what to do?
> > 
> > Perhaps we can start a new internet project "vocabulary research":
> > 
> >   - We try to find a system for word building (like the list in one of
> >     my previous mails, but better.)
> 
> We can do that as a Re-Neo-Glosa experimental project.
> All living languages evolve, and we can help that process.
> Lojban implemented a plan for future growth and reform.
> Occidental is inherently mutable to adjust to user needs.
> To freeze any language is either impossible or detrimental.

However, languages really change through real use.  Artificial
languages seem to be somehow a prey to tinkerers (funny word) with
bright ideas not forged in real use, and not contributed by means
of a demonstration in real writing.  At the same time they (I mean
IALs) do not have the centre of gravity and the total weight of
usage which would allow them to shrug off attack, as it were.

Thus I suggest you need to be careful not to slip into the notion
of a freely reformable IAL.  It exists as defined, or it doesn't
exist.

Date: Sun, 27 Aug 2000 08:18:53 +0930 (CST)

At 2000-08-26 11:06 -0700, Charles:

> To freeze any language is either impossible or detrimental.
> 
> >   - Everybody can help then.
> 
> Radicals and reactionaries and moderates can all help.
> We should make one restriction though, once we get organized:
> all discussion about change must be in standard Glosa.
> The first step is to clean-up the standard dictionary,
> without any reforms, just collection and corrections.
> Then we can try using the language, with translation
> in English or some other. That's my suggestion.

Another comment, if this is about fundamental reform.  A Tetum
grammar by Geoffrey Hull will shortly be published, or so I have
read.  Not being a real student of these matters (I'm just
interested in a language for e-mail) I don't know how common
published grammars of anciently creolized languages are.  Nor how
useful the contemplation of one would be for Glosa developers.

But in case you are interested, here is a quote from the
Introduction to Hull's Tetum - English dictionary.

"... Tetum (like the other eight Austronesian languages of the
island, except Lovaia) descends from a language introduced from
the Buton region of south-eastern Celebes about a thousand years
ago.  Invasive Austronesian speech displaced the older Papuan
vernaculars of Timor ... and subsequently underwent the processes
of drastic structural simplification and restructuration which
linguists call pidginization and creolization...  Today Eastern
Tetum is a hybrid ... heavy Portugese superstratum.  The impact of
Portugese ... may be aptly compared to the massive influence of
French on English after the Norman Conquest."

Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: final vowels
From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 26 Aug 2000 19:45:03 -0400 (EDT)

On Sat, 26 Aug 2000, Charles wrote (excerpt):

> Too many collisions between common Latin words, e.g.:

    However, Hogben's original Interglossa was mostly Greek-based, and
a lot of the Glosa lexicon is also.  Thai's Frater leaned a little more
toward Latin than Glosa does, but Glosa has more Greek leanings.  I
know that some are in favor of Latinising WENSA-based* IALs in general,
but I am not convinced that a conIAL targeted at non-WENSAns also need
be entirely Latinate.  Now some might argue that without the WENSAns a
conIAL cannot take off, and that therefore they must be accomodated
first and foremost, but I am not convinced.  (However, I admit to some
slight personal bias toward Greek.)

> No ... final vowels are always unstressed,

    Perhaps this is a bias of native anglophones?  After all, I very
much doubt that an IAL intended for worldwide use can be everything to
everybody.  Why should structure always be biased in favor of
anglophone sloppiness?  Esperanto and Ido seem to get along well using
unstressed (by definition) final syllables as part-of-speech markers.

> and rounded lips look cute on chicks. But Euros
> don't like -u much, except maybe in Rumania.

    See above.  I am not convinced that Euro habits must be the sole
determinant.

> > - Species names keep nominativus (equs, ursus).
> 
> [...]
> The correct terms are binomial, not just "Ursa".

    What, then, would be a generic term for "bear," to include black
bears, sun bears, grizzly bears, and polar bears?

> All languages are unsystematic compromises, even Lojban, even
> predicate calculus, even Java. But some are simpler and better
> than others. I hear the linguistic police now at my door ...

    Indeed.  I am wary of purism in IAL design.  There are always
trade-offs.  That is why, for example, 'ge-' does not offend me.

=====
* For those not familiar with it, Leo Moser coined the acronym WENSA
to represent the majority languages of:
W estern
E urope
N orth and
S outh America and
A ustralia [and New Zealand, I add]

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: final vowels
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sat, 26 Aug 2000 18:41:13 -0700


> > No ... final vowels are always unstressed,
> 
>     Perhaps this is a bias of native anglophones?

Nay, it is *already* officially part of the basic phonology
of Glosa to stress penultimately, not bias. I think Glosa
di-syllabic words sound very cool, like Bantu languages,
but many tri-syls are iffy, and there are too many mono-syls.

> > The correct terms are binomial, not just "Ursa".
> 
>     What, then, would be a generic term for "bear," to include black
> bears, sun bears, grizzly bears, and polar bears?

I shoulda used a better example: "Felis"; "feli" is a good
common word. All words in Glosa *should* end in vowels
(just my opinion, not a fact). The authors capitalize
species names because they are not part of normal vocab.
My point was that Glosa is no-way scientific, and those
ugly species names break from the predominant sound-pattern.
How about "Albertosaurus"?? "Buckminsterfullerene"????

> I am wary of purism in IAL design.  There are always
> trade-offs.  That is why, for example, 'ge-' does not offend me.

How about a present/active participle to go with that?
The "ge" has proven itself useful and somewhat popular.

From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>

[About Charles's http://catty.com/~catty/lang/glosatest.dat ,
to the dictionarys on my site www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406 ]

Good: 
  Your glosatest.dat is a useful help for pointing out typographical 
  errors and for choosing Glosa synonyms. 

Not so good: 

 > >  > There are 8567 items in total, 5638 appear only once,
 > >  > 1401 appear twice, 1528 more than twice.
 > > 
 > > So, you think the 1401 should be the new "Core" and the
 > > 1401+1528 the new "Base"?
 > 
 > Yup!

  When you compare 5 different "Glosa 1000"s, you may get a 
Super-1000, but no real advance.  We cannot take glosatest.dat for 
making new "Basic" and "Core".  We would 

- destroy Bill's work!
    (That would be sad.  Bill did a good job.  It is only that we have 
    now a better data set.)
- get a lot of (bad and useless) synonyms! 
    (That was what Bill, Paul and Robin wanted to avoid.)
- and get other useless and unimportant words!


Examples: 

  We will have "aerodroma" (you marked with "3") in "Core" and "Basic" 
and "aeroporto" (2) in "Basic".  Or "aeroplana" (3) and 
"aeroplano" (2). Totally useless and bad synonyms.  But see Bill's 
word list.  He eliminated these synonyms already.  Let us not go back. 

  Glosa "adeno" is English "gland".  "gland" is not in your 
vitaless-list nor in Kilgariff-3000.  So "adeno" is not very necessary, 
but you marked it with "3" and it would come into the new "Core". 



  I think, the task remains (although I hope for other suggestions): 

- Put the "+"s and "++"s to the "1"s - manually!  And then, perhaps 
  after that, perhaps simultanously 
- Reduce amount of words in "Core" and "Basic"!  It is no perfect way 
  to take an English list for it, but your 
  http://catty.com/~catty/lang/vitaless.txt and Kilgariff-3000 would 
  be acceptable.

ab            absent; away; from; off   [not 'absenti']
alexi-baci    bumper     [?fender ~ Is this a type of 'stick'?]
algo soni     groan; moan   [=3Dalgo-voci   whine]
ali           any   [=3Duno   some]
ali kron      ever  [=3Dali-kron   whenever]
alo...alo     either...or   [=3Dalo..alo either]
ambi          both   [=3Dpan]
ante meso-di  a.m.  [should be ~pre meso-di~]
aparatu       tackle [=3Dnece-ra]
apo           subtract; whisk         [=3Dapo G    remove; skim]=20
                       [whisk=3Dbrush away lightly]
aqa tera      marsh  [=3Daqa-landa    swamp]
arane         spider web [=3Daranea-reti   spider-web] (should be =
~arakni-reti~)
arde minus flama=20
              smoulder  [should be ~piro minus flama~]
artisti       artist   [~arti-pe~]
asinu         ass   [=3Dasinus    donkey]
atenda        await   [=3Datende     delay; wait]
Avena         oat   [=3Davena   porridge] (~plu avena~)

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 27 Aug 2000 07:39:26 -0700


>   When you compare 5 different "Glosa 1000"s, you may get a
> Super-1000, but no real advance.  We cannot take glosatest.dat for
> making new "Basic" and "Core".  We would
> 
> - destroy Bill's work!
>     (That would be sad.  Bill did a good job.  It is only that we have
>     now a better data set.)
> - get a lot of (bad and useless) synonyms!
>     (That was what Bill, Paul and Robin wanted to avoid.)
> - and get other useless and unimportant words!

Good points. Mere counts are not sufficient.

> - Put the "+"s and "++"s to the "1"s - manually!

I could add the source-list for each word automatically,
e.g. if Bill's list was A, G1E5 was B, etc:

ABCDE   5    razo    graze, scrape, shave
^^^^^

Manual editing is error-prone and not fun! I looked at
the GRA- words last night and started categorizing:

2       grata   scrape, scratch
1       psesto  scraped
1       radula  scraper
1       raspa   scrape
5       razo    graze, scrape, shave
1       razo-me razor
1       exkoria scrape skin off
1       abrazi  scrape

That's a lot of synonyms.

1       grati           grateful
5       gratia          grace, thank, thanks
2       gratioso        graceful
1       gratia de       thank you for
1       gratia-ple      grateful, thankful
1       gratia-ra       reward
3       gratula         congratulate
3       gratis          free, free of charge, without paying
1       kongratula      congratulate
1       ingratia-se     ingratiate oneself
1       disgratia       disgrace

This shows some of the "crypto-fixes".

3       seba        grease, suet, wax
4       lipo        fat, fry, grease, lipid
2       grasi       fat, grease
1       pingui      fat
5       paki        coarse, fat, stout, thick
3       obesi       fat, obese
3       krasi       fat, thick
1       fliaka      fathead
1       adipo       animal fat
1       paki-fi     cable, cord
1       paki-karni  steak

The authors were obsessed with dieting?

3       grafi           draw, write
5       grafo           draw, script, write, writing
1       grafo de        describe
1       grafo-liqi      ink
1       grafo-me        typewriter
1       grafo-pe        author
1       grafo-ra        stationery
1       grafo-ru        pen, pencil
1       graft           graft
1       fikti-grafo     fiction
1       brevi grafo     essay
1       bio-grafo       biography
1       foto-grafi      photograph
1       foto-grafi-me   camera
1       fotografi       photograph
1       ge-grafo akorda contract
1       geografi        geography
1       kako-grafo      bad writing
1       pornografi      pornography
1       paragrafo       paragraph
1       topografi       topography
1       takografo       shorthand, stenography
1       tako grafi      sketch

There's a model word-family.

1       grana           grain
1       granari         granary
1       granata         pomegranate
1       pomegranata     pomegranate
2       granita         granite
1       granula         granule
1       holo grana      wholemeal

All of these share the same basic root, "grain".

I think Glosa needs a consistent derivational paradigm,
and some clear strategy for final vowels. Natural langs
are chaotic, but a designed IAL should not be so.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: final vowels
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 27 Aug 2000 07:49:37 -0700


> > How about a present/active participle to go with that?
> > The "ge" has proven itself useful and somewhat popular.

> The present/active participle is just the unmodified word itself. There's no
> need to complicate matters here by adding a redundant feature.

What's redundant? If I write "edi kani" or "kani edi"
it is very unclear what it means. Any verb used as
an adjective will benefit from "ge-" and a proper
active participle. In the official materials, "nu-"
is sometimes used as such, but this overloads the more
usual meaning. If "pa-" is different from "ge-", then
we need something different from "nu-" for the other.
Alternatively, perhaps someone can cite *any* language
with one participle and not the other?

 > ante meso-di  a.m.  [should be ~pre meso-di~] 

  I recognized it already, too.  The idea was certainly to keep the 
English abbreviation "am". 

  What to do with such words?  Should we simply write 
"pre meso-di   a.m." and put "ante meso-di" into the "Literary" 
dictionary?  Or is it better to mark them (e.g. 
"pre meso-di *    a.m.") as not original GEO?  This is certainly a

original.  But how to decide it by a computer program?  Is it better 
to correct the list before or to make first a new "Core"-dictionary 
and have then the advantage to work on a limited amount of words?

Glosa (perhaps because you read "Plu Glosa Nota").  If criticism gives 
that feeling, it could be easily accepted by Wendy, I think.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] repair crypto-prefixes?
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 28 Aug 2000 11:31:59 -0700

I preformed the following easy experiments,
showing how both Glosa and English unsurprisingly
incorporate a huge number of prefixed words
following the Latin pattern of preposition+root,
e.g. "rebel = re + bell". The most common ones
seem to be "con- re- in- de- pre-".

Taking the first 2 letters of all common English words ...
cat /usr/dict/words | grep '^[a-z]' | cut -b -2 | sort | uniq -c  | sort -n | tail

    582 ca
    618 un
    625 su
    755 st
    866 di
    923 pr
    952 de
   1307 in
   1718 re
   1793 co

Now for Glosa. Only keeping the 10 most popular combinations ...
cut -f 3 glosatest* | cut -b -2 | sort | uniq -c  | sort -n | tail

    136 te
    142 pe
    165 re
    167 me
    170 pa
    173 ma
    180 st
    195 pr
    265 ka
    339 ko

Glosa vocab as listed in existing glossaries (Glosaries?)
is not as systematic as in Latin or English, but *is*
somewhat easily fixable. The result could be a much smaller
and more coherent/powerful and mnemonic vocab. For example,
consider all the Glosa "KO" words:

Some of them are already of the form "KO-etc":
ko-co ko-existe ko-habita ko-hesio ko-labora ko-lekti ko-opera ko-ordina ko-proletari

Some are "KONTRA-etc":
kontra kontra-akti kontra-co kontra-dice kontrabanda kontradice kontraface kontrakti kontramanda kontrapunkti kontrari kontrari-pe kontrasigna kontrasti kontraveni

Anyway, I propose that we identify all KO- words better written
as "KO-etc" or "KONTRA-etc" then list them that way. Correct
pronunciation would stress both KO and first syllable of root.
This makes derivation both transparent and *productive*,
for free generation of natural understandable compounds.

This would clean up the new Glosa dictionary and break no rules.
One problem: some of the separate roots are not defined, though
obvious enough and easy to add. For reference, here are all 339
official KO- words:

echo `cut -f 3 glosatest.dat | grep '^ko'`
