koagula koalesce koano koaxa kodi kodoni koelo koeno koerce kogita
kognati kognato kognoske koincide koitus koko koku koketa kokix koklea

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>
Date: Mon, 28 Aug 2000 23:31:27 +0200

  Thank you, Charles, for writing your UNIX-inputs.  I always admire 
UNIX-jugglers (and GNUplotters, too).  I have not so much practice 
with "uniq" etc and pipes.  Then it is good to have some examples. 


  I wrote a little program.  It brings out a lot of nonsense and has 
errors, yet.  But perhaps it could help for my/our problem 
(change +/++ to 1s in www.tu-harburg.de/~smms1406/gglen.htm ). 
Its output looks like this: 

...
---------------
  19 abjekti          mean 

4548 meandro          wind along 
4684 meskino +        mean; paltry 
5159 non-amo 1        disgust; mean {not nice} 
5649 per 1++          by means of; per 
6826 semani 1++       mean {to mean}; meaning 
6920 signifi 1++      mean; signify 
7682 tem-co +         meanwhile; time {at that} 
8441 volu akti 1      mean {v} 
8442 volu dice 1      mean {v} 
mean            : Paul; Rick; Adam 301 
---------------
  20 abjura +         abjure 

abjure          : 
---------------
  21 ablati           remove 

 498 apo 1++G         apart; away; remove; skim; subtract; whisk 
1381 depila +         remove hair 
6519 remove ++        remove 
remove          : Paul; Rick; Adam 2926 
---------------
...

  (Explanation: Numbers are line numbers in gglen.txt file.  So they 
could be found easily with a text editor. 
The program takes one line, e.g. line 19 with "abjekti".  It takes the 
English meanings, here only one, "mean".  Then it searches through the 
whole rest of the file for all English meanings of abjekti and prints 
out the lines (with line numbers), where it founds them. 
After that it gives to every English word the information, whether it 
is in Paul Bartlett's list, in Rick Harrison's list and on which 
position it is in the BNC of Adam Kilgariff. 
Then the program takes the next line (here line 20 with abjura).) 

  How to work with the list:  e.g. "ablati" remains only in the 
"Literary"-dictionary, because there is a synonym ("apo") in the 
"Core" already.  The Glosa word "remove" will be removed from "Core", 
because in "Core" there is already "apo", and "apo" has a "1", so it 
is in Glosa 1000 and has to be prefered.  "depila" should move to 
"Literary", too. 
The comment "Paul; Rick; Adam 2926" shows that the word "remove" is so 
important (it is in all three lists), that it should remain in "Core". 
Otherwise it would move to "Basic".  

  I do not believe that a deeper computerization makes sense. 
There is too much to correct in the data 
(mean {to mean} ---> mean {v}, ...).

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 28 Aug 2000 16:56:20 -0700


> UNIX-jugglers (and GNUplotters, too).  I have not so much practice
> with "uniq" etc and pipes.

Those utilities, e.g. sort uniq grep cut vi, are for easy things;
but for everything else: perl and its associative arrays!

> all English meanings of abjekti and prints
> out the lines (with line numbers), where it founds them.

grep 'abkekti' filename

> After that it gives to every English word the information, whether it
> is in Paul Bartlett's list, in Rick Harrison's list and on which
> position it is in the BNC of Adam Kilgariff.

I don't trust the English words given as equivalents.
Better to use the Greek or Latin whenever possible ...
or Ido, because it fonetisized the vocabulary similarly.

>   How to work with the list:  e.g. "ablati" remains only in the
> "Literary"-dictionary, because there is a synonym ("apo") in the
> "Core" already.

I think it is much more important whether the Glosa word
is used in compounds, e.g. BATE:

24      bat       bata       bat
12345   bat       bate       batter, beat, hit, knock, smite, strike, thrash
4       bat       bate-tem   innings
45      bat       bati       deep, walking
145     bat       bato       deep, depth <----------------- different root
1       bat       ne-bate    miss
15      batal     batalia    battle
5       batal     batalo     hind part <------------------- useless root
245     bater     bateri     battery, utensils <----------- ???
5       batiment  batimento  beating, throb
15      batm      batmo      step <------------------------ different

In this case our methods agree, since all the dictionaries
think BATE is important enough to list. But in others ... ?

> The comment "Paul; Rick; Adam 2926" shows that the word "remove" is so
> important (it is in all three lists), that it should remain in "Core".
> Otherwise it would move to "Basic".
> 
>   I do not believe that a deeper computerization makes sense.

But REMOVE is not a good root at all ... it is a hidden compound
of RE + MOV, and MOV does not appear as a proper root anywhere!

5      ablat       ablati    remove
5      ambit       ambiti    moving around perimeter
12345  ap          apo       apart, away, remove, skim, subtract, whisk
1      cinem       cinema    cinema, motion picture theater, movie theater
5      depil       depila    remove hair
5      et          kin-eti   moving
12345  k           ki        go, move
2345   kin         kine      go, motion, move, move oneself, whisk
4      kin         no-kine   motionless, not moving, stationary, still {not moving}
15     kines       kinesi    motion, moving
2345   mot         moti      drive, lay, move, move something, place, put, shift, transfer
4      moviment    movimento traffic
15     remov       remove    remove

Here one can see many crypt-affixes. MOVE should be the root,
RE-MOVE, MOVIMENTO (MOVE-AKTI?) are derivatives, and others
(*MOMENTUM?) should also be buildable using other good Glosa roots.

I think we need to write them as RE-MOVE, AB-LATI, DE-PILA, etc,
so we can catch those horrendous etymological errors. I am not
worried about too many synonyms, because the popularity of each
word is best judged by the number of vocab-lists it appears in.

But I might reduce the *English* synonymns to 2 or 3 max per G word.

It's good to discuss these things: sometimes you convince me,
sometimes I convince myself ...

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] question
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick_hempshall{at}hotmail{dot}com>
Date: Tue, 29 Aug 2000 08:07:06 +0100

Alexander E. Kirpichev grafo


> Saluta, karo plu Glosa-pe!
> Why current dictionary of Glosa has many words which don't necessary?
> For example, GENE SKO - learn, APRENDE - learn, MATi -learn.

Glosa has 2 different word-lists CENTRA and MEGA. CENTRA GLOSA has only
~GENE SKO~ as part of its list; ~APRENDE~ and ~MATI~ belong to the larger
MEGA GLOSA, and are available for those who feel the need to use them.

> Maybe necessary to use words, teatra, artisti etc. which are
International.
> But some words are excrescent!

CENTRA GLOSA is the heart of the language, but all the international words
are part of Glosa, and all the Greek and Latin elements that make up the
Sci-Tech  vocabulary. This results in far too many words for every day use
and our current dictionary project is trying to correct this.

CENTRA GLOSA has about 1200 "concepts" and 1800 words, with some "concepts"
represented by both a Greek and a Latin word. This seems to be sub-optimal,
but it has some advantages. Some people on the list prefer to use the Latin
word and some prefer the Greek one. We can choose for ourselves which we
use. People remember more words they use, so CENTRA GLOSA works with human
language skills. We only need to use about 1200 words and to recognise in
context about 600 additional synonyms.

> We don't need in adaptatio and in ADAPTA both, we must select.
> (My choose is adaptatio - adaptation, to adapt, etc. And what is your
> choose?)

~ADAPTA~ is the CENTRA GLOSA word.


Nick

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 29 Aug 2000 09:55:52 -0700


> I followed just YOUR own idea.

But I keep changing my ideas.

> And I find it is useful.

Yes, one can always learn much from the mistakes of others.

(Smiley picture goes here.)

>   glosaref.dat and classif.dat can only differ from each other and from
> g1e5.dat by errors of the Glosa authors, because all three should be
> Glosa 1000.
> 
>   glosaref.dat and classif.dat have been sources of Bill's dictionary
> and should be completely included.  When you take gec.dat, you compare
> the same with the same.
> 
>   You get only negatives (errors) and no positive information.  It is
> like an experiment in physics, where somebody takes the noise of the
> amplifyer for the result (happened to ME once).

But where they agree, we have more reliable data.
So we can know theat CANCRI is probably wrong in its
final vowel, because the other sources disagree.
The union of these overlapping files is the basic vocab.
Then we have all the rest, which is the Glosa-6000
plus a lot of noise.

>  > 24      bat       bata       bat
> 
>   I wondered often, what this word means:  a stick, racket (sport)/
> to beat/ the animal.  From the classical origin all is possible.
> Please tell, if you know.  I needed it for the Glosa-German-dictionary.

The most important words are always verbs, because they generate
whole families of terms. BATUARE was Latin for "to beat, batter,
repeatedly smack the stuffing out of something"; this led to
Vulgar BATTERE, leading to English "battle, battalion, abate,
combat, debate, rebate, abbatoire", ad infinitum.

So for super-productivity and its converse minimal-vocabulary,
we need a root BATE and the prefixes RE- AB- COM- etc.
Imagine a matrix/table of 20 prefixes and 50 roots; that's
1000 verbs if fully populated, each of which can have
10 further derivatives (10,000 words) by participle and such,
yet only using 50 + 20 + 10 = 80 morphemes ... Nice, huh?

might think it's too close to Kosmoglotta.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 29 Aug 2000 14:01:47 -0700


> The most important words are always verbs, because they generate
> whole families of terms. BATUARE was Latin for "to beat, batter,
> repeatedly smack the stuffing out of something"; this led to
> Vulgar BATTERE, leading to English "battle, battalion, abate,
> combat, debate, rebate, abbatoire", ad infinitum.
> 
> So for super-productivity and its converse minimal-vocabulary,
> we need a root BATE and the prefixes RE- AB- COM- etc.
> Imagine a matrix/table of 20 prefixes and 50 roots; that's
> 1000 verbs if fully populated, each of which can have
> 10 further derivatives (10,000 words) by participle and such,
> yet only using 50 + 20 + 10 = 80 morphemes ... Nice, huh?

I think I didn't completely answer the question ...
I'll add on a few things here:

The authors of Glosa have (unconsciously?) included all
the standard super-productive Latin roots and prefixes
and many of the suffixes, but in a hidden manner.
Words like "REMOVE" are really "RE-MOVE", etc, and when
written with the hyphen, it is easy to sort such words
by the root (MOVE) rather than the prefix (RE) and then
suddenly all the word families become obvious. A good
example is SIGN ...

5       sign       a-signa   assign
145     sign      de-signa   design
4       sign      in-signi   ensign, flag
1245    sign      in-signia  badge, banner, flag
5       sign  kontra-signa   countersign

I am tired already, doing this manually. When all the
true "IN-" etc compounds are flagged as such, a program
will sort them all automatically.

>From such super-productive verbs are *also* derived
a huge number of useful adjectives, nouns, etc.
Not syntactically, but semantically. To "beat = BATE"
with something, a "bat = BATE-ME" (or something similar)
is useful. Normally one would use Romance "-ERO" etc.
and you can find various "-IA, -IO, -ISM" etc in Glosa.
There are many other such useful suffixes *already*
embedded in the Glosa vocab waiting to be dug out, but
they aren't as obvious or consistent as the prefixes.

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick_hempshall{at}hotmail{dot}com>

Charles grafo (mero)

> 45      bat       bati       deep, walking
> 145     bat       bato       deep, depth <----------------- different root

bati  = bathy- = deep, depth . bathysphere [From Greek bathus 'deep']
bato = batho- = deep, depth . bathometer [From Greek bathos 'depth']

I see no reason not to treat these two words as one Glosa word with
acceptable variation in "euphonic" terminal vowel.


Any idea where ~bati~ = waking comes from? I note that your file also has
~badisti~ = walker. Is there a common "root" there?

> But REMOVE is not a good root at all ... it is a hidden compound

The CENTRA GLOSA equivalent would be ~re-moti~, which would mean "move back
to beginning". Glosa does have the "international" prefixes as part of the
language, but gives them a specific meaning, rather than the vague meanings
of the natural languages. IMO only compounds that make semantic sense are
useful; the rest of the 'hidden compounds' (for example ~kolekti~) should be
treated as indivisible, unanalysable units.

Nick

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Tue, 29 Aug 2000 16:39:41 -0700


> bati  = bathy- = deep, depth . bathysphere [From Greek bathus 'deep']
> bato = batho- = deep, depth . bathometer [From Greek bathos 'depth']

> Any idea where ~bati~ = waking comes from? I note that your file also has
> ~badisti~ = walker. Is there a common "root" there?

I think the "walk" idea is just a mistake, so if corrected there
should be just one root. For the sake of a respectable dictionary,
only one vowel ending per word should be kept, I don't know or
care which; there seems no particular pattern to them.

> > But REMOVE is not a good root at all ... it is a hidden compound
> 
> The CENTRA GLOSA equivalent would be ~re-moti~, which would mean "move back
> to beginning".

Yes, Glosa has MOTI as well as MOVE. But consider all the possible
derivatives and ease of use ... "rebate, debate, ..." and also
"motor, motive, ...". Occidental accepts MOVE- as infinitive
and MOT- as perfective forms, then derives the family.

> Glosa does have the "international" prefixes as part of the
> language, but gives them a specific meaning, rather than the vague meanings
> of the natural languages.

Glosa is the same as Latin: "cum" can be a (relatively) specific
particle, while "con-/com-" are vague prefixes. I am ambivalent
about this practice, but it's not my choice, they did it!

> IMO only compounds that make semantic sense are
> useful; the rest of the 'hidden compounds' (for example ~kolekti~) should be
> treated as indivisible, unanalysable units.

I disagree: LECT is one of the important crypto-roots:
"select, collect, delect, elect ...". Each of those has further
sets of derivatives. It's designed deeply into the system.

the Greek word.  We still cannot communicate, the very condition Glosa
(or any other auxiliary language) was supposed to ameliorate.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] sur/epi
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Thu, 31 Aug 2000 09:49:45 -0700


Some people dislike the Latin prefixed vague prepositions,
and find them inconsistently used and silly in Glosa,
e.g. "su facia, su-facia, surfaci, surfacia" = "surface",
and "superficia" = "superficial". I mean the whole set of
"ab- ad- de- con- pre- pro- re- sub- sur-" etc.

There may be a usable alternative in the Greek prefixes, e.g.
"dia, epi, exo". They better fit the disyllabic sound pattern
and eliminate that just-another-Romance-interlingua feeling.
I am wondering if it would be worth the effort to search
systematically for replacements, e.g. SURFACIA --> EPIFACI.

This requires mixing Greek prefixes with Latin roots,
and moves away ("ki ab") from "international vocabulary"
and at-sight recognizability. But it seems more in
accord with the original justification for Interglossa,
scientific Greek vocabulary with simplified syntax.

As it is today, I can't see how to use Glosa seriously,
it's too much of a mish-mash. I re-sorted the vocab
roughly by roots and find things like this everywhere,
even basic stuff like "make, build, face":

faba            bean
faber           artisan
fabrika         structure
fabula          myth, tale
bene-face       do good
lega-face       legislation
face            build, construct, make, manufacture, produce
face-do         factory
faci            making
facia           face, features, surface
facu u neo ra   invent
ge-face         artificial, produce
ge-face ex      consist
no-face         demolish, destroy, havoc, undo
su facia        surface
su-facia        surface
faceta          facet
faceti          witty

So now I don't know whether to consider Glosa as possible
euro-pidgin (bene-face, lega-face, fabri-domi, epi-face)
or anglo-latin (beneficial, surface, facetious, invention).
I'd really like to know how others see it ...

I don't have a problem with ~a.m.~ = ~pre meso-di~ or even = ~mana~. "a.m."
doesn't abbreviate any English words, and I guess this is the case in most
languages where it occurs. I guess we should be marking any changes from the
GEO standard, until these can be approved.

varying word lists. So to me, it makes perfect sense to correct the typo's,
smooth out the differences, and establish a common standard. I doubt we need

the G1KE5K file.

Charles grafo

> an adjective will benefit from "ge-" and a proper

A parallel English example would be "meeting eating" or "eating meeting".
Only a larger context would explain what was intended.

~Kani vora.~                                     Dog(s) is/are/were/will be
eating.
~Vora kani ne es mi more.~               Eating dogs is not my pratice. Dog
eating is not my practice.
~Kani, qi vora, es de mi.~                  The dog(s), which is/are eating,
belong(s) to me. The eating dogs belong to me.
~Galina ne es boni kaso kani vora~    Chicken isn't good as a dog meal.
(~vora~ = eat = eating = a meal)

Nick

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: dictionary
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Thu, 31 Aug 2000 13:09:18 -0700


> > LECT is one of the important crypto-roots:
> > "select, collect, delect, elect ...". Each of those has further
> > sets of derivatives. It's designed deeply into the system.
> 
> I think these root families are very interesting, but I don't see how the
> meanings of the words are related in present day Glosa. They feel like they
> represent distinct "concepts" to me.

In a sense they are distinct concepts. The average person cannot
predict that "sit/stand + again/back" would mean "stay/remain".
Yet that is how Latin and Glosa have chosen: RESID and RESTA.
I would have expected "MANA = stay" for Glosa, but instead
"MANA = morning". So it is clear that Glosa accepts the Latin
word building system of a prefixed vague adverb with a root:
EMANAte, IMANE=in+mana, PERMANEnt. 

But in a deeper sense there is intuitive "natural" logic
in this system. From a small number of basic verb roots ("trunks"),
the first branches will tend to follow the lines of multiplicity
or repetition or unification or metaphorical motion toward/away.
Those are the RE- KO- etc directional prefixes, and Greek uses
them too, and so do all the "international words" ... no escape!

There were very similar processes in the Amerindian languages.
I urge skeptics to closely examine the combined Glosa vocabulary.
What I at first thought was dispensable in the Glosa-6000 words
turns out to be the most essential.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: final vowels
From: Charles <catty{at}pacificnet{dot}net>
Date: Thu, 31 Aug 2000 13:25:34 -0700


> A parallel English example would be "meeting eating" or "eating meeting".
> Only a larger context would explain what was intended.

But "larger context" is never a satifactory solution in any
designed language. Even a pidgin quickly evolves an idiom
to express frequent concepts. [I reformat examples below.]

> ~Kani vora.~                  Dog(s) is/are/were/will be eating.

Is that "dog eats" or "dog's food" or "dog voraciousness"?
Glosa has no morphological part-of-speech distinctions,
so "kani vora" is a valid (but vague) noun phrase.
Thus it confuses possessives, gerunds, verbs, whatever.
I would not accept your sentence as valid except as
a "telegraphed" form of "u kani pa/nu vora".

> ~Vora kani ne es mi more.~    Eating dogs is not my pratice.
>                               Dog eating is not my practice.

Vora kani es u menace.          Voracious dogs are a menace.

> ~Kani, qi vora, es de mi.~    The dog(s), which is/are eating, belong(s) to me.
>                               The eating dogs belong to me.

That is clear but too clumsy for common use. Glosa needs
both a clear possesive and a present participle.

> ~Galina ne es boni kaso kani vora~    Chicken isn't good as a dog meal.
> (~vora~ = eat = eating = a meal)

That could also mean "chicken isn't the average dog's eating".
I think these examples have helped prove the point ...
